v
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
Abstract
DTU Compute
Mathematical Modelling and Computation
Real Time SURE Image Processing
by Sebastian Kazmarek Præsius
A GPU accelerated SURE image processing pipeline was created as part of this thesis. It was
demonstrated that real-time beamforming and image processing could be achieved, but not
both at once with the device used. An eﬃcient GPU implementation of spline interpolation
was created, which approximately matches MATLABs interp2 in output, but is able to
execute many times faster on the device used. A high performance beamformer was produced.
The majority of time was spent on these parts, and the resulting code can be used not just
for SURE imaging, but has many applications beyond SURE imaging.

1
Chapter 1
Introduction
1.1
Purpose
The purpose is to port matrix Matlab code to execution on a high-end GPU for real time
SURE imaging. The code should be able to run under Matlab interfaced to the Verasonics
research scanners for real time imaging in a clinical setting.
1.2
SURE Imaging
SUper-Resolution imaging using the Erythrocytes as targets (SURE) is a new method to
perform super-resolution ultrasound imaging (SRI). Instead of injecting and tracking sparse
microbubbles in the blood vessels, as in conventional SRI , the erythrocytes are targeted [1].
There will be many erythrocytes within any given resolution cell, but a given peak detec-
tion algorithm will still detect a peak lying inside the vessel in this case [1].
Before detection can be performed, the tissue signal must be removed. One could for
example subtract the average of image values to remove the background – but the issue
is that tissue is not completely stationary, and may move with things like breathing and
heartbeats, or appear to move due to probe movements.
Therefore a motion estimation
and image alignment is performed. This means that the SURE imaging pipeline consists
of beamforming, motion estimation and correction, stationary echo cancellation, and ﬁnally,
peak detection.
1.3
Introduction
Graphics Processing Units (GPUs) are specialized for high-throughput. Real-time is in this
thesis taken to mean a display of 60+ frames per second, and a delay of less than 20 millisec-
onds, essentially a high-throughput, low-latency implementation is needed. SURE imaging
requires an even higher frame-rate to perform motion estimation, around 400 images per
second for the dataset used throughout the implementation.
The largest bottleneck was spline interpolation, for which an eﬃcient GPU implementa-
tion was made. Then the MATLAB peak detection code made by Jørgen Arendt Jensen
was ported to GPU. The quadrature processing was simpliﬁed. Later this was formalized
mathematically, which brought some further simpliﬁcations, leading to a massive increase in
performance in this part. After all of this, the image processing attained real-time speeds.
Finally, the beamformer was the slowest part, where most of processing time was spent.
The majority of development time was spent on optimizing the beamformer, with the most
impactful and interesting optimizations given in the beamforming chapter. The novel beam-
former and spline implementations could be of use not just in the SURE project.
Implementations were written in MATLAB, C++ and Compute Uniﬁed Device Architec-
ture (CUDA). The latter is a language used for the creation of GPU programs.
==PAGE:20==
2
Chapter 1. Introduction
1.4
GPU Processing
The main principles will be outlined using CUDA terminology. CUDA kernels (programs)
are usually launched asynchronously, meaning they are scheduled for execution on the GPU
without blocking the calling CPU program. A user-speciﬁed number of threads will then
execute the same kernel process, potentially in parallel.
Figure 1.1: Organization of threads. Image from [2].
Kernel launch happens on a grid, which is divided into thread blocks of up to 1024 threads,
which are executed by the cores. The threads in each block are then ﬁnally divided sequen-
tially into warps of up to 32 threads, which execute the same instruction simultaneously.
Therefore the block dimension should be a multiple of 32, to avoid inactive threads in warps.
Grid and block dimensions must be speciﬁed at kernel launch, and can be given in up to 3
dimensions, which can be useful for handling image and volumetric data. The reason for this
organization is essentially to abstract away the underlying hardware.

==PAGE:21==
1.4. GPU Processing
3
1.4.1
Architecture
The hardware is characterized by a Compute Capability (version), and the cores are called
Streaming Multiprocessors (SMs). For example, the RTX 3090 has a compute capability
of 8.6 and 82 SMs with 4 warp schedulers each, and can then perform 82 · 4 · 32 = 10496
operations per clock cycle; its base clock frequency is 1395 MHz, leading to a processing
power of up to 14.64 · 1012 operations per second, resulting in up to 29.28 teraFLOPS1.
As can be seen in table 1.1, the speciﬁcs vary between compute capabilities. The term register
will be used for 32-bit registers, unless otherwise mentioned. The term ﬂoat will be used for
single precision ﬂoating-point numbers. Floats are 32-bit; it takes one register to store a ﬂoat.
 
Figure 1.2: The GA10x SM used in the RTX 3090. Image from [4].
1Multiplication and addition can be done as one operation, thereby doubling the count of ﬂoating-point
operations.
==PAGE:22==
4
Chapter 1. Introduction
Throughput
Table 1.3 gives a categorization of operations, and table 1.1 gives their throughput.
Type
Example of operation
ALU
Integer addition and multiplication.
Bit-wise logic operations.
Comparison, minimum, maximum.
FPU
Float addition and multiplication.
SFU/XU
Approximations to reciprocal, reciprocal square-root,
base-2 logarithm, base-2 exponential, sine and cosine.
Conversion between integer and ﬂoat.
Table 1.3: Examples of types of operations on 32-bit operands.
The square root is implemented as a reciprocal square-root followed by a reciprocal. [3]
Double precision ﬂoating point numbers will not be used in this thesis – there are just
two units per SM for this, leading to 1/64th the throughput compared to FPU ops: "The
small number of FP64 hardware units are included to ensure any programs with FP64 code
operate correctly [...]" [4].
As can be seen in ﬁg. 1.2, the throughput appear to be directly related to the number of
each type of unit (ALU=INT32 cores, FPU=FP32 cores). The load store (LD/ST) units are
for memory instructions, and texture units (Tex) for textures.
Latency
Instructions are also characterized by their latency, meaning the number of cycles before the
result can be read. This is for the most part undocumented, but for compute capability 7.x
it is conﬁrmed to be four clock cycles for some FPU instructions [5]. Papers have performed
micro-benchmarks to estimate the values, see the table below for an example of values.
Operation
FPU/ALU
SFU
Shared
memory
L1 hit
L2 hit
L2 miss
Latency [cycles]
4
≈15
19
32
≈188
≈616
Table 1.4: Latencies on a T4 GPU (compute capability 7.5). Data from [6].
Instructions are pipelined, meaning another FPU instruction can be issued while one is al-
ready being processed2. For an FPU instruction with 4 cycles of latency, at least four other
instructions need to be executed to hide its latency.
For example, this can be done using instruction-level parallelism (ILP):
Listing 1.1: Example of instruction-level parallelism
1 Thread 1
2 x = x+a;
3 y = y+b;
// No stall, as this instruction is independent from the first.
4 z = z+c;
// Still no stall.
5 w = w+d;
// Full throughput is reached with 4 operations "in-flight".
6 //w = w+z;// This line would have to wait for the previous two to complete.
In each clock cycle the scheduler selects and issues the instruction of an eligible (ready to
execute) warp. With up to 12 warps per scheduler with compute capability 8.6 (table 1.1),
this means that independent instructions can come from threads in other warps.
2There are limitations for speciﬁc instructions. Some low-throughput units can get throttled. For example,
device memory access are put in the L1 instruction queue, which is of a ﬁnite size; resulting in stall if full.
==PAGE:23==
1.4. GPU Processing
5
Listing 1.2: Example of thread-level parallelism.
1 Thread 1
Thread 2
Thread 3
Thread 4
2 x=x+a;
3
y=y+b;
4
z=z+c;
5
w=w+d;
// Full throughput is reached.
The state of a warp is either selected or stalled, and if all active warps are stalled in a cycle,
resources are potentially being under-utilized.
Memory
Compute capability 7 and above feature a uniﬁed data cache. It is a conﬁgurable partitioned
memory, with one part being shared memory and the other the L1/texture cache. There
is one L1 cache per SM, while the L2 cache is shared between SMs. By default, read and
written data will be cached in L2 (making changes visible across the grid), and read-only
accesses may be cached in L1. The device memory (RAM) is most abundant, but very costly
to access. It has a lower throughput and higher latency than the other types.
Memory must be accessed through a hierarchy, such that e.g. a L1 cache miss results in
an L2 memory request. The organization of memory with a compute capability 7.5 is shown
in ﬁg. 1.3, but 8.6 uses the same organization [3]. Processing blocks are warp schedulers.
Private to every GPU 
Private to every SM 
Private to every processing block 
64 KiB registers
16 KiB L0 instruction cache
96 KiB L1 data cache/shared memory
2 KiB L1 constant cache
~46 KiB L1.5 constant cache/L1 instruction cache
4096 KiB L2 data cache/L2 constant cache/L2 instruction cache
~16 GiB GDDR6
Figure 1.3: Memory hierarchy of a T4 GPU (capability 7.5). Figure from [6].
Constant caches are where among other things, the function arguments are cached – instead
of each thread needing to hold an individual copy, wasting registers. Registers are the fastest
type of memory (virtually no latency), but it is not dynamically addressable. Instead, static
references to speciﬁc registers are part of the instructions.
==PAGE:24==
6
Chapter 1. Introduction
1.4.2
Programs and optimization
Instructions can be predicated with a per-thread boolean value, meaning they are scheduled
for execution, but only those with a true predicate are actually executed; those with a false
predicate do not write results, evaluate addresses, or read operands.
Warp divergence can happen when a predicated branching instruction is encountered.
If subsets of the warp proceed through diﬀering branches, each path of the branch must be
executed (masking out threads not participating in the path), serializing an otherwise parallel
warp execution. The compiler will ultimately decide if predication or true branching is done.
In general, the optimizing compiler is allowed to interpret code in any way that preserves
program semantics, and it will often do so to minimize branching and maximize potential
latency hiding. For example it will re-order instructions to promote instruction-level paral-
lelism, and it will usually move device memory instructions as early as possible.
Global memory fetch operations will not stall the thread immediately, but the thread
will encounter a long scoreboard stall when it attempts to read the output register of the
operation before it has completed. This makes it more diﬃcult to pin-point the cause when
proﬁling. Similarly short scoreboard stall appear to happen for variable-latency instructions
that are computed inside the SM, e.g. shared-memory access and SFU operations.
CUDA devices have a fused multiply-add (FMA) instruction, which can compute x ←
x + y · z as a single operation, rounding once. The compiler will by default merge multiplies
and adds, which changes program semantics, but does so in a way giving more precision.
The optimizer can be conservative if hints are not given, or due to unintentional semantics
or dependencies. Some examples:
1. By default, if data is written to a pointer, any read operation on any other pointer
cannot be moved before this write, as these may happen to point to the same location.
Decorating pointers as read-only with const __restrict__ can help solve this. [3], [7]
2. The short-circuit logical AND, &&, does not evaluate the second operand in case the
ﬁrst is false. This will usually be handled by predication using the ﬁrst operand. In
many cases a bit-wise AND, &, will be faster, as no ordering is imposed.
3. Floating point arithmetic is not associative, so summations can not freely be re-ordered.
4. Loop unrolling is unlikely to be performed in case there is branching inside the loop,
this is because the unrolled loop can contain exponentially more branches.
Loop unrolling is an important optimization which will often be done automatically.
It
reduces loop overhead, and gives more instructions to re-order.
There is a trade-oﬀ, as
unrolling requires more registers (e.g.
each read operation reserve a register), increasing
register pressure. Since the amount of memory is ﬁxed, this can result in a smaller thread
pool, reducing occupancy, and therefore reducing the potential for latency hiding.
==PAGE:25==
1.4. GPU Processing
7
1.4.3
Compilation
The Nvidia CUDA Compiler (NVCC) is used to compile the C++-like language, CUDA, to
Parallel Thread Execution (PTX) instructions. PTX is a well-documented instruction set for
a virtual machine with arbitrarily many registers, used as an intermediate representation.
Listing 1.3: Example of a mapping between CUDA code, PTX, and SASS.
// CUDA code:
array[0] = 0;
// PTX (for virtual architecture compute_52, meaning it targets sm_52):
mov.u32
%r1, 0;
st.global.u32
[%rd2], %r1;
// SASS (for real architecture: sm_86, meaning Compute Capability 8.6):
STG.E [R2.64], RZ;
For execution, PTX will eventually be translated into Streaming ASSembler (SASS) instruc-
tions for the speciﬁc Compute Capability of the GPU. This can be done by the graphics
driver, allowing forward compatibility of PTX to future devices.
The processing units in a GPU are relatively simple compared to a CPU; instead there
are many of them. Register assignment is done statically in SASS. Inspecting proﬁled SASS
can then be useful, but may risk tuning the program to a speciﬁc GPU at the expense of
others – but that holds for any code optimization.
1.4.4
CUDA in MATLAB
MATLAB stores GPU data as gpuArray objects. Many MATLAB functions have a GPU
implementation, which is then used if the input argument is on the GPU. This means many
operations can be performed without even having to write any CUDA code.
% Example of ways to initialize GPU data, and compute something on the GPU.
x = ones(100, 1, 'gpuArray');
% Create array of ones on GPU.
y = ones(1, 100);
z = y * x;
% Compute this product on GPU. (implicitly: z = gpuArray(y) * x)
For higher performance, there is in some cases the option of using the function arrayfun,
which will turn a given function handle into a kernel, which is executed on the GPU. Then
loops can run on the GPU, and multiple operations can be done in a single kernel launch.
z = arrayfun(@kernel, x, y);
% Compute z on the GPU.
function result = kernel(a, b)
% The arguments are always scalar.
result = a * b;
end
The features of gpyArray.arrayfun are limited. If more ﬂexibility or performance is required,
one can use the parallel.gpu.CUDAKernel interface.
This can take a .ptx ﬁle (compiled
kernel) and execute it on a GPU with gpuArray inputs. One can conﬁgure kernel parameters
such as the launch grid, and most CUDA language features are available.
Finally, if a CUDA feature is not supported by CUDAKernel, or a library such as cuBLAS
is needed, there is the option of creating a MEX (MATLAB executable) from C/C++ code,
e.g. compiled with mexcuda. These can be run as if they are MATLAB functions. Then any
C++/CUDA language features are available. MEX ﬁles created this way will usually contain
pre-translated SASS for multiple architectures. This solution is not portable in general.
The cost of a higher ﬂexibility solution is usually more development time.

Chapter 2
Beamformer
2.1
Introduction
Most time was spent implementing the new beamformer.
Complex RF data and single
precision was used. The previous beamformer, from [8], was designed for Row-Column probes,
but works with 1D probes for 2D imaging as well. All distance calculations are therefore done
in 2D, which saves some processing time when 3D imaging is performed.
2.2
Theory
Beamforming involves synchronizing the data received by the transducers with respect to a
time-of-ﬂight, such that sensitivity for a speciﬁc point in space is maximized. The time delay
between a receiver position prx and some point p is:
τ = ∥prx −p∥
c
,
(2.1)
where c is the speed of sound, assumed constant.
A single receiver will not be able to
distinguish direction, as the delay is constant in the sphere: {p | τ}, as seen in ﬁg. 2.1.
Figure 2.1: Example of a B-mode (log-compressed) image produced by a single
transducer. Simulated with Field II [9] [10].

Figure 2.2: Example of a B-mode image produced using delay-and-sum with 128
receivers. Simulated with Field II [9] [10].

where ri : R →R is the signal received by transducer i. The delay essentially maps the raw 1D

constructive interference of the mapped signals, but it can produce undesired "sidelobes" as
seen in ﬁg. 2.2, which can be reduced with appropriate apodization ai.
Figure 2.3: Example of summing single element emission images with dynamic
apodization. Simulated with Field II [9] [10].
Often multiple elements are used to transmit a pulse in unison by applying appropriate
transmission delays (and apodization), so as to synchronize the pulse arrival times in a
single point, called the focal point. By distributing the focal points uniformly in the lateral

2.3
Implementation
2.3.1
Initial implementation
The previous beamformer from [8] accumulates a single LRI pixel per thread onto an given
input image. Creating an HRI then simply means running the beamformer Nt times.
Algorithm 1 Outline of previous beamforming algorithm from [8]. "Heavy" operations are
counted on the right. See appendix C.1 for the implementation.

The RF data array rij[] is of shape [Nt, Nr, τ max] (with leading dimension τ max). Care was
taken to access data in a well-optimized manner [8]: Contiguous threads produce pixels
along arbitrary lines in space. For SURE the lines were oriented axially, such that τ usually
increases monotonically within a thread block. The result is strided access, with a variable
stride. Proﬁling will reveal if this is an issue; it may even be that the stride on average is less
than 1. According to the discussion in [8]: "The main bottleneck of the CUDA beamformer
is thus the latency of individual memory accesses".
==PAGE:31==
2.3. Implementation
13
2.3.2
Initial proﬁling
Grid conﬁguration
From an initial proﬁling of the generation of a single LRI with algorithm 1, using NVIDIA
Nsight Compute to proﬁle the code, we get a few warnings.
The block size warning (ﬁg. 2.5) states: "Threads are executed in groups of 32 threads
called warps. This kernel launch is conﬁgured to execute 936 threads per block. Consequently,
some threads in a warp are masked oﬀand those hardware resources are unused. Try changing
the number of threads per block to be a multiple of 32 threads. Between 128 and 256 threads
per block is a good initial range for experimentation. Use smaller thread blocks rather than
one large thread block per multiprocessor if latency aﬀects performance. [...]". For smaller
blocks there can be a higher occupancy, and therefore better latency hiding.
Figure 2.5: Block size warning from NVIDIA Nsight Compute.
In this case Nz = 1871, and then the work was split into two blocks of size 936 (max block
size is 1024), for an even workload, as discussed in [8]. But this means one warp will have
only 8 threads. We change the original code (appendix C.1):
// Figure out which sample to calculate
const unsigned int line_idx = blockIdx.x;
const unsigned int sample_idx = threadIdx.x + blockIdx.y*blockDim.x;
To use a more conventional grid layout (appendix C.2):
// Figure out which sample to calculate
const unsigned int sample_idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;
const unsigned int
line_idx = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y;
In this case the block size is ﬁxed, and the grid size is made to ﬁt the data. In MATLAB:
Listing 2.1: Launch conﬁguration used throughout most of the implementation.
% n_samples_per_line = Nz = 1871
% n_lines = Nx = 337
kernel.ThreadBlockSize = [16 8];
kernel.GridSize = [ceil(n_samples_per_line/16) ceil(n_lines/8)];
The grid is conﬁgurable to have a block write a rectangular patch in the beamformed image,
which gives an opportunity to tune to the speciﬁc beamforming geometry and image size. For
example we want the delays τ between threads in a block be close, such that the up to 32 · 4
loads (algorithm 1 line 9) are covered by fewer cache segments.
Code

After
After
After
After

registers used with 2D blocks, which may be why [936, 1] is slower.
There was an 18%

==PAGE:32==
14
Chapter 2. Beamformer
Good cache use
Proﬁling shows an L1 cache hit rate of 90%, and L2 hit rate of 97%. This means there is
not much traﬃc which has to go all the way to the device memory. Only 8.33 MB of data is
fetched from main memory, and then 575 MB is eventually read through the L2 cache.
Uncoalesced Memory Access
NSight Compute warns about uncoalesced global accesses on line 14; when the second grid
dimension is above 1 there can be uncoalesced read/write at this line. But it is in this case
acceptable, since only few reads and writes are done there. There are warnings about the L1
and L2 cache access patterns as well when the data samples are loaded.
Using shared memory as a managed L1 cache can often be an improvement to this, but
it is not advisable to synchronize the whole block inside of the main loop (required to use
shared memory on line 9 in algorithm 1), as the slowest warp will then slow the whole block.
If there are few enough receivers, Nr, it is possible to place all their positions in shared
memory at the start of the kernel, and then load from there on line 5, giving a speedup; but
it can result in lowered occupancy, so other optimizations were applied ﬁrst.
Latency issues
NSight Compute did not give warnings about latency, but inspection of the source-proﬁling
(when compiling with the -lineinfo ﬂag) and SASS showed there was indeed much stall
due to memory access. Figure 2.6 was created from 6167 samples of warp states at times
where no instructions were issued, meaning all warps in the scheduler were stalled. As shown
in ﬁg. 2.6, empirically it appears 49% of time full SM stall was due to long-latency instructions,
11 % due to short-latency instructions and 30% due to ﬁxed-latency instructions. Proﬁling
showed that on average no instructions were issued 19% of clock cycles, due to stall.
Figure 2.6: Reasons for failure to hide latencies.
Note that stall does not paint the full picture. Executing any instruction takes time, meaning
any unnecessary instructions reduce throughput as well. Stall is simply wasted clock cycles.
A great amount of time is spent with Wait. This is natural; even a kernel with only short
latency instructions will have many warps waiting, but it is usually hidden by other warps
executing, essentially taking turns, and then the SM will not stall as a whole. But since many
threads are "long/short scoreboard" stalled, there is a failure to hide these short latencies.
==PAGE:33==
2.3. Implementation
15
2.3.3
First Optimizations
Simple optimizations, requiring only few or no changes to the code.
Compiler ﬂags
Applying the compiler ﬂag -use_fast_math reduced the execution time to only 930 usec.
It is not uncommon to trade precision for performance. Commonly, a norm is computed:
float norm = sqrt(x * x + y * y);
Where loss of precision can happen depending on the magnitude of x and y. The CUDA/C
function hypot(x, y) does the same, but with a precision guarantee over the full range of
inputs. It is signiﬁcantly slower, and often not used.
Both division and square root are computed as a sequence of instructions to ensure IEEE
754 compliance [3]. They initially use a hardware-accelerated value, which is then reﬁned.
With the ﬂag -use_fast_math, the initial values can be used directly. This can also be
done manually in other ways. And the ﬂag also enables other changes. See [14] for more
information. We will analyze if any signiﬁcant error is introduced in the results section. The
original beamformer had a running time of 1.07 msec, when compiled with this ﬂag.
Loop unswitching
Inside of the apodization functions there is a check if the F-number (the proportionality
constant in eq. (2.7)) is zero.
This value is constant throughout the kernel launch, but
inspection of the SASS revealed the check is performed every iteration of the inner loop. We
can conclude NVCC did not perform loop unswitching, which is a simple optimization where
versioning of the loop is done. An example:
// Before loop unswitching
for (int i=0; i < Nr; i++)
do_work();
if (rx_f != 0)
do_more_work();
And with unswitching, moving the loop-invariant control-ﬂow condition outside the loop:
// With loop unswitching
if (rx_f == 0)
for (int i=0; i < Nr; i++)
do_work();
else
for (int i=0; i < Nr; i++) {
do_work();
do_more_work();
}
Doing an optimization like this manually can be prone to error. One solution is to hint at
the compiler to do a versioning of the loop for us, placing the loop in an inline function:
// Hint at the compiler to do unswitching.
if(rx_f == 0)
// The compiler will realize rx_f is zero in this branch;
// it will then remove the now redundant check inside the loop.
function_with_loop(rx_f)
// <- function containing the original loop.
else
// The compiler will realize rx_f is non-zero in this branch.
function_with_loop(rx_f)
==PAGE:34==
16
Chapter 2. Beamformer
The compiler picks up on the hint, and moves the branching outside the inner loop.
A
larger PTX/SASS binary size is produced, which in most cases is not an issue. With this
optimization, along with the --use_fast_math ﬂag, the execution time became 911 usec.
These optimizations completely removed branching instructions inside the loop.
Early exit outside acceptance region
From algorithm 1 it is clear that the majority of time is spent in the inner loop, as the number
of receivers Nr is 168 for this data. By exiting early if atx = 0, a large amount of unnecessary
work is avoided; and if it is zero for the whole warp/block, resources are freed up.
Similar for arx, but in this case it is best to simply evaluate if the normalized apodization
index (eq. (2.7)) falls outside of the range [−0.5, 0.5], in which case there is no need to load
the two values needed for linear interpolation of apodization values.
The result is that an early continuing in the loop (see algorithm 2 line 10) saves 6 LOADS
whenever it is performed. A branching instruction was inserted by the compiler, avoiding the
many unnecessary instructions. "check_rx_apodization_nonzero" does one division. 1

2.3. Implementation
17
2.3.4
Software Pipelining
For every iteration of the inner loop in algorithm 2, a division and load operation is performed
to evaluate the early continue condition. The load must be completed before the division can
start, so we have at least Nr long and short scoreboard stalls in sequence.
Unrolling the loop is not possible due to the branching on early continue. Otherwise this
could help give the load-instructions more time "in-ﬂight", before the eventual stall. Another
out-of-order execution method is software pipelining [15]. An example without pipelining:
// Without software pipelining.
for (int i=0; i < Nr; i++) {
float delay = tx_delay + norm(rx_pos[i]-img_coord);
do_delay_and_sum(delay);
}
And then with a two-stage pipeline, needed to hide the latency of the two sequential stalls:
Listing 2.2: Example of software pipelining in C.
1 // With a pipelining: delay <- one_forward_norm <- two_forward_rx_pos
2 float2 two_forward_rx_pos;
// Costs two registers
3 float one_forward_norm;
// Costs one register
4
5 for (int i=-2; i < Nr; i++) {
6
float delay = tx_delay + one_forward_norm;
7
one_forward_norm = norm(two_forward_rx_pos-img_coord);
8
if (i+2 < Nr)
9
two_forward_rx_pos = rx_pos[i+2];
// This gets delivered the next iteration.
10
11
if (0 <= i)
12
do_delay_and_sum(delay);
13 }
This is generally combined with unswitching leading to up to three diﬀerent loops: A prologue
for ﬁlling the pipeline, the original loop body going from 0 to Nr −2 without need for any
range checks, and ﬁnally an epilogue, where the upper range checks here must be performed
to prevent an access violation. The prologue and epilogue can then be unrolled.
The loading of rx_pos[i+2] is done last in the code of the pipeline for readability and
correctness, but the compiler will most likely move it to the beginning of the loop. Then
every operation after, including do_delay_and_sum(delay) can help hide its latency.
Since register assignment is static, it is not straightforward to pipeline instructions over more
than one iteration; see [16] for more information.
# Stalls  # Registers

Compute(A); A=READ(3);
Compute(A); A=READ(4);
Compute(A);

B=READ(2);

^ Epilogue ^
Figure 2.7: A timeline illustration of how pipelining minimizes stall (yellow). It
is assumed Compute(..) takes enough time to hide latency from one READ(..).
Pipelining of the Lagrange interpolation and sample loading was attempted, but abandoned
due to complexity. The square-root is not necessary to evaluate the early continue condition
due to eq. (2.10), but as a speculative execution, it was faster to have the value ready in case
early continue was not done, to prevent a stall. The three necessary heavy operations of
square-root, division and load were pipelined, producing a runtime of 588 usec.
==PAGE:36==

Chapter 2. Beamformer
2.3.5
Various
By applying various optimizations, a greater performance gain was realized.
FMA for Linear Interpolation:
Inspired by a blog from NVIDIA Engineer Mark Harris [17]. Before, the linear interpolation
compiled to an addition2, multiplication and FMA instruction:
rx_apo_val = frac_index * rx_apo[s0+1] + (1 - frac_index) * rx_apo[s0];
But it can be rewritten to just two FMA:
// Do linear interpolation as two fused multiply-add operations:
rx_apo_val = frac_index*rx_apo[s0+1] + (rx_apo[s0] - frac_index*rx_apo[s0]);
The with this change, the runtime was 551 usec. A similar modiﬁcation was made for the
mapping from normalized apodization index to apodization index, giving a time 540 usec:
apo_index = apo_index + 0.5; // Move to interval [0, 1].
float sample_index = apo_index * n_samples_rx_apo - apo_index;
int s0 = floor(sample_index);
Shared memory:
Loading the values, prx
i , of the rx_pos array into a shared memory array of size Nr +2 gave
a speedup. This is done in the beginning of the kernel by:
1 #define N_RX_POS_MAX 168
2 // Load into shared memory the rx positions.
3 __shared__ float2 smem_rx_pos[N_RX_POS_MAX+2];
4 for (int i = threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; i < n_rx_pos;
5
i += blockDim.x * blockDim.y)
// Grid-stride loop.
6
smem_rx_pos[i] = rx_pos[i];
7 __syncthreads();
8
9 // Now simply overwrite the pointer with the one to smem.
10 rx_pos = smem_rx_pos;
By requesting space for two additional unused values in shared memory, an access violation
can be avoided without the pipeline epilogue (listing 2.2). The epilogue was removed, giving
a runtime of 510 usec.

2.3. Implementation
19
Signed integers
The use of unsigned integers was replaced with signed versions. As stated in the CUDA
C Best Practices Guide, signed integers can be more aggressively optimized; their overﬂow
result in undeﬁned behavior, meaning the compiler can assume it does not happen.
With this change, the runtime was 485 usec.
Absolute comparison instruction
For the receive apodization check, early continuing was done if for the normalized apodization
index in CUDA: !(-0.5 <= apo_index & apo_index <= 0.5). The resulting SASS:
#
Source
target: sm_86
150 FSETP.GT.AND
P4, PT, R9,
0.5, PT
151 FSETP.GEU.AND P2, PT, R9, -0.5, PT
...
157 PLOP3.LUT P2, PT, P4, P2, PT, 0x8, 0x0
_____________________________________________________________
RX: Register
PX: Predicate register, with PT being True
FSETP: FP32 Compare And Set Predicate
PLOP3: Predicate Logic Operation
One can see last instruction was placed later by the compiler to account for latency.
It was discovered to be faster to check abs(apo_index) <= 0.5, which appears to have
hardware support, mapping to a single instruction:
#
Source
150 FSETP.GTU.AND P1, PT, |R9|, 0.5, PT
Note the absolute value on register 9! FSETP and PLOP3 functionality is undocumented.
One might expect the compiler to perform this optimization, given both formulations are
semantically equivalent, but it did not. With this change, the runtime became 480 usec.
Figure 2.8: The stall reasons after various optimizations. Based on 1859 samples.
From ﬁg. 2.8, it can be seen that short scoreboard stall was signiﬁcantly reduced. Loading
shared memory is a short scoreboard operation (as shared memory resides in the SM), but
its latency is well hidden with software pipelining. Overall, no instructions were issued only
10% of clock cycles, according to statistical proﬁling with NVIDIA NSight Compute.
With all the optimizations, the kernel executed 3.1 times faster than initially: 480 usec.

Chapter 2.
Beamformer
2.3.6
Micro-batching
Batching is a technique commonly used in GPU processing. For example, if a single workload
too small to saturate the GPUs potential for throughput, one might group multiple uniform
workloads together and complete them all in a single kernel launch, which can then be done
faster than executing each kernel individually.
A sequence of Nf HRIs are computed by a deeply nested loop. To illustrate:
1 for (int k=0; k < Nf; k++)
// CPU: The loop to create an image sequence
2
for (int j=0; j < Nt; j++)
// CPU: The loop over LRIs to create a HRI
3
for (int i=0; i < Nr; i++) { // GPU: Calculation of some LRI
4
float delay = tx_delay[j] + norm(rx_pos[i]-img_coord);
5
pixel_in_image[k] += do_delay_and_sum(delay, rf_data[i, j, k]);
6
}
There is a nested loop on the CPU, which launches kernels to accelerate the innermost
loop by executing it on the GPU. For most beamforming use, consecutive HRIs have the
same conﬁguration. This means the GPU will be computing the same delays and dynamic
apodization values every frame. Batching frames can therefore be used to amortize the cost
of these calculations. First some theory.
As in loop nest optimization, using the "loop blocking" technique [18], the loop:
for (i=0; i < N; ++i)
can be tiled/blocked with a block size B as:
for (j=0; j < N; j+=B)
for (i=j; i < min(j+B, N); i++)
It should be clear that i still spans {1, 2 . . . , N} in the same order as before. The beneﬁt
is that now the inner loop has a ﬁxed size (max B), which allows a host of optimizations.
For example, one could load a suitably sized block into shared memory, and work on this.
Assuming B divides N, we get the invariant min(j+B, N)=j+B. Then deﬁne b by:
i < min(j + B, N) = j + B ⇒b := i −j < B
(2.11)
Such that i = b + j = b + batchN, where batch is simply the number of iterations of the
outermost j-loop. The batched loop is then the transformation of the blocked loop:
for (batch=0; batch < N/B; batch++)
for (b=0; b < B; b++)
i = batch*B + b;
Now for the application. With this in mind, we can rewrite the image sequence loop:
1 for (int batch=0; batch < Nf/B; k++) // CPU: Loop to create the HRI sequence
2
for (int j=0; j < Nt; j++)
// CPU: Loop over LRIs to create a HRI
3
for (int i=0; i < Nr; i++) {
// GPU: Calculation of some LRI
4
float delay = tx_delay[j] + norm(rx_pos[i]-img_coord);
5
for (b=0; b < B; b++) {
6
int k = batch*B + b;
7
pixel_in_image[k] += do_delay_and_sum(delay, rf_data[i, j, k]);
8
}
9
}
Listing 2.3: How images are made with microbatching.
A loop interchange is performed, moving the b-loop to the deepest nesting level. A large
stride is introduced in rf_data in our case, but the beneﬁt is that almost all other variables
depend only on i and j (e.g. delay), so they can be left out of the new innermost b-loop.
==PAGE:39==
2.3. Implementation
21
% n_samples_per_line = Nz = 1871
% n_lines = Nx = 337
% n_seq_elems = Nt = 12
kernel.ThreadBlockSize = [16 8 1];
kernel.GridSize = [ceil(n_samples_per_line/16) ceil(n_lines/8) n_seq_elems];
Listing 2.4: Example of kernel launch conﬁguration for beamforming one HRI.
In fact, a conventional grid is in the form of a batch operation, (see listing 2.4), where the
assumption that B (here 16, 8 and 1) divides N (here Nz, Nx, Nt) is dropped by taking
ceil(N/B), as well as checking that i < N, which in essence is the check that most kernels
start out with. The batches (blocks/tiles) are then distributed among SMs in the GPU.
In our case the assumption that B divides Nf is retained, meaning the new innermost
loop has a static iteration count, making it easy to unroll. And B becomes the unrolling
factor, which is heavily limited by the number of registers, which is why we use the term
micro-batching for this optimization. Diﬀerent versions of the kernel can then be made for
diﬀerent B using C++ templates. For odd inputs, e.g. B = 4 and Nf = 7, the fastest
solution would be to simply launch a version of the kernel with B = 3 for the ﬁnal batch.
The main idea of the micro-batching algorithm
Every thread will compute B sums instead of one. These B sums have almost every variable in
common (e.g. delay and apodization values); therefore the cost of computing these variables
is amortized by a factor B, and only a few more registers are required.
As discussed in section 2.3.2, the beamformer has very good cache behaviour on the
speciﬁc example input.
SASS proﬁling revealed that only the very ﬁrst use of a sample
caused signiﬁcant long scoreboard stall; after this, the three last samples appeared to not
stall at all. This means micro-batching may even be able to reduce the long scoreboard stall
somewhat, as 4B memory requests can then be made at a time.
Testing the idea
To allow the micro-batched versions to amortize the load instructions, enough registers must
be available. NVCC can be aggressive in lowering register count, favoring a higher occupancy
in most cases. As a solution, the kernel can be decorated with
__launch_bounds__(128, 6)
Which speciﬁes a maximum number of threads in a block of 128, and a minimum of 6 warps
per multiprocessor3. This corresponds to an occupancy of 50%, or a register count of 80
(ﬁg. 2.9), on the 8.6 compute capability device used.
Figure 2.9: The relation between occupancy and register count for the kernel.
(Graph produced using the NVIDIA Nsight Compute Occupancy Calculator)
With these launch bounds, the PTX to SASS compiler is given explicit permission to increase
register count (up to 80) to hide single thread latency. This is required for micro-batching to
3There are 4 multiprocessors (warp schedulers) per SM for Compute Capability 5.x, 6.1, 6.2, 7.x and 8.x.
==PAGE:40==
22
Chapter 2. Beamformer
work optimally. For example, with B = 5 and complex input, 5·4·2 = 40 registers need to be
reserved for the output of the 5 · 4 sample loads, if they must all be "in-ﬂight" simultaneously.
In this experiment, the same LRI beamforming conﬁguration as was used throughout
this section had been used, but the B sets of four consecutive samples used in Lagrange
interpolation had a stride of NtNrτ max (between sets), and the ﬁnal writes with a stride of
NxNz (between batches). Diﬀerent launch bounds were tried, with results shown in table 2.3.

One could compile all versions with

shown. For B = 24, using a higher number of registers gave a worse performance.
For real-time beamforming at a rate of 400 HRIs per second, 400Nt = 4800 LRIs must
be computed per second, which corresponds to a runtime ≤208 usec per LRI.
Batching
introduces a delay, as B frames of data must be ready to be processed at a time. This is
impractical, and therefore B = 4 will be used, leading to a delay of ≤4/400 sec = 10 msec.
Figure 2.10: There are diminishing returns to using greater batch sizes.
The pipelining optimization becomes much more important at low occupancy since there are
fewer resident warps to help hide the latencies; it was also developed chronologically after
micro-batching, as were most of the other optimizations. The open-source project cuTLASS
uses low occupancy along with pipelining and other techniques to achieve a high-performance
GEMM [19], and proﬁling showed the cuBLAS GEMM functions have low occupancy as well.
Using B = 4, the runtime was then 205 usec per LRI. Without launch bounds, the time
would instead be 215 usec per LRI, with 48 registers used, giving a higher occupancy.

Eﬃcient batched interpolation
The Lagrange interpolation is computed given four consecutive samples y0, y1, y2, y3 as:
2"rij(τ)" = x1x2(x0y3 −x3y0)/3 + x0x3(x2y1 −x1y2)
(2.12)
With x0 = τ −k (τ, k from algorithm 1), x1 = x0 −1, x2 = x0 −2, x3 = x0 −3.
Equation (2.12) can be written as a dot product:
2"rij(τ)" =


−x1x2x3/3
x0x2x3
−x0x1x3
x0x1x2/3

·


y0
y1
y2
y3

= w · y
(2.13)
When batching, many operations can be saved by pre-computing the weights w. This is a
function of the delay, and can thus be shared over the micro-batch. The cost of computing
w can be used to hide some of the latency of the many load operations. The interpolations
can then be computed as a sequence of mainly FMA operations.4 This was done as:
1 // Let sum[batch_size] be the array of micro-batched sums.
2 // Let samples[batch_size][4] be the array of loaded samples.
3
4 // Compute the common interpolation weights.
5 // Used in case of N>1. This way the cost is amortized over batch_size elems.
6 float x0 = total_dist - rx_sample_idx, x1 = x0 - 1, x2 = x0 - 2, x3 = x0 - 3;
7 float w03 = x1*x2/3;
// Division here compiles to multiplication by 1/3.0f.
8 float w12 = x0*x3;
9 float w0 = -w03*x3,
10
w1 =
w12*x2,
11
w2 = -w12*x1,
12
w3 =
w03*x0;
13
14 for (int b = 0; b < batch_size; b++)
15
// Interpolation calculation.
16
if (batch_size > 1)
17
sum[b] += rx_apo_val * (samples[b][0] * w0 + samples[b][1] * w1
18
+ samples[b][2] * w2 + samples[b][3] * w3);
19
else
20
sum[b] += rx_apo_val * cubic_interpolation(samples[b][0], samples[b][1],
21
samples[b][2], samples[b][3],
22
total_dist-rx_sample_idx);
Where the evaluation of "if (batch_size > 1)" is done at compile-time, and if false, lines
6-12 are removed by the optimizer. The arrays sum and samples are stored in registers.
With this change, the runtime was 773 usec, 193 usec per LRI.
Atomic accumulation
Atomic accumulation is required for concurrent summation of LRIs (e.g. listing 2.4) in order
to prevent race conditions. This is because the an accumulate x ←x + a expands to the
three instructions. To illustrate, we essentially have:
// The line "pixel_in_image[k] += do_delay_and_sum(delay, rf_data[i, j, k])":
float tmp = pixel_in_image[k];
tmp = tmp + do_delay_and_sum(delay, rf_data[i, j, k]);
pixel_in_image[k] = tmp;
4A stream of FPU operations has maximum throughput on compute capability 8.6 speciﬁcally.
==PAGE:42==
24
Chapter 2. Beamformer
Each of the threads could in theory read the same initial value, with the result that they all
write their own version of tmp, giving an incorrect summation. The CUDA function atomi-
cAdd is used to serialize the read-modify-write operations, producing a correct algorithm:
atomicAdd(&pixel_in_image[k], do_delay_and_sum(delay, rf_data[i, j, k]))
atomicAdd essentially performs the summation as if it was one single (atomic) instruction.
The additional time-cost with atomicAdd turn out to be insigniﬁcant. Writes happen so
infrequently that its unlikely the operations were not already serial by chance.
The implementation is found in appendix C.6. The runtime is still around 773 usec.
Asynchronous data transfer
For the SURE data, the frames are interleaved sequences. De-interleaving was performed
before beamforming, but the time cost was signiﬁcant. It also takes approximately as long
to move data to the GPU as it does to perform the beamforming now.
Instead, the beamforming kernel can simply be fed data in the raw (interleaved) ordering,
but supplied with a diﬀerent output array depending on which of the HRI the interleaved
LRI data belong to. This was not implemented in the interest of time.5
The CUDA Streams API allow for concurrent execution of kernels as well as memory
transfers between the host and device. This way computations can overlap with each other,
and with host to device (H2D) memory transfers. This was implemented in C++ as a MAT-
LAB executable (MEX) ﬁle (appendix C.7). Up to 128 kernels can execute concurrently with
an 8.6 compute capability (table 1.1), but 2 streams turned out to be suﬃcient, essentially
performing a double-buﬀering.
Figure 2.11: The two CUDA streams, performing data transfer and compute
concurrently. (Timeline generated with NVIDIA NSight Systems)
Note there is almost no overlap between kernels on the two streams in ﬁg. 2.11.
Since
kernel execution takes approximately as long as the data transfer, concurrent kernel execution
may not be necessary. MATLAB already supports overlapping data transfers with kernel
execution, but not overlapping kernels with kernels. This kernel sums 12 · 4 LRIs at a time
to produce the 4 HRIs in a batched manner. The resulting rate is given in the next section.
5The interleaved implementation using the conﬁguration in listing 2.4 was made prior to simpliﬁcations.

of interleaved frames). This corresponds to 197 usec per LRI.

the transfer time to get data onto the GPU.

The kernel is likely less eﬃcient than feeding data in interleaved ordering to the previous,

This can possibly be computed on the CPU as samples arrive, before transferring to GPU.

It can be seen that the arithmetic intensity (FLOP per byte from device memory) is by far
the lowest for the B = 16 batched kernel, being approximately 1/16th that of the version
without batching (unbatched). More cycles are spent with the scheduler being stalled (No
Eligible %) for the batched versions, likely due to lower occupancy.
Any warp that has an instruction issued spends one cycle being selected (it is issued this
cycle). The unbatched version has a high occupancy, so many warps spend their time simply
not being selected (only 1 can be selected out of at most 12), which is usually ﬁne.
Figure 2.13: The SASS sampling (for B = 4) shows the proportion of time spent.
==PAGE:45==
2.4. Results
27
Figure 2.13 was created from a proﬁling with NVIDIA Nsight Compute. The instruction
counter of warps (which instruction #) was sampled at regular intervals, then these counts
were normalized by the total, which gives an empirical time spent on each SASS instruction.
In ﬁg. 2.13 it can be seen how the instructions in the inner loop are more costly (in-
structions #146-281). The FMA and multiplications instructions after the data has been
loaded (#229-270) are very cheap considering they are performed Nr = 168 times as well.
This makes sense, as with the Ampere architecture (compute capability 8.6), these have full
throughput, theoretically 29.28 teraFLOPS. The preceding data load instructions (#188-212)
account for 27% of samples. And then, the ﬁrst time one of these samples are used is on
instruction #228, where 17% of time is spent, mainly due to long scoreboard stall.
Figure 2.14: Memory chart for B = 4, produced with NVIDIA Nsight Compute.
The implementations are most likely not yet limited by memory bandwidth. The limit for
this device was 29.28 teraFLOP/s, which with a device memory bandwidth of 936 GB/s
corresponds to a memory-bound region of arithmetic intensities below 31.3 FLOP/byte. All
the versions, except B = 16, thus perform too many arithmetic operations to be bound by
device memory bandwidth. The B = 16 version is possibly memory-bound, but the reason
may be ineﬃcient use of memory (high L2 miss), since it only had a 16% increased imaging
rate compared to the B = 4 version.
==PAGE:46==

the "eﬃcient batching interpolation" result in diﬀerent rounding errors from before, which
should be investigated. In this subsection we compare the output of the new beamformer
with the compiler ﬂags, to the original beamformer, compiled without compiler ﬂags.

2.4. Results
29
the pixel-wise diﬀerence between the two outputs (shown in the bottom right plot). The
log-compression in the data distributions was done using as reference 0 dB ≡5.17·105, which
is the largest absolute value of the output of the original beamformer.
Real input
Throughout the implementation, the beamformer was tested with and optimized for complex
input, as analytic representation was done before beamforming. Complex input results in a
twice the register pressure from the 4B loaded samples and the B sums.
The kernel templated on input type, meaning no code changes are needed for real input.
Using real input with B = 4 results in a runtime of 180 usec per LRI, and 56 registers used.
Using B = 16 for the real input gives a time per LRI of 136 usec (corresponding to 603
HRI/s) with 80 registers, and if 115 registers are used instead, the performance is slightly
decreased. Note the large improvement in the B = 16 case. This is likely because the data
better ﬁts in the L2 cache, reducing costly L2 misses.
Diﬀerent output sizes
The pixels positions are computed from a format of lines with a given origin and direction. It
is then simple to reproduce the same geometry, but with a diﬀerent resolution. This is done
by changing the axial or lateral sampling rate while keeping the lattice end-points ﬁxed.
Figure 2.17 shows the result of changing both the axial and lateral resolution by some
factor. The measurement was repeated 10 times (shown in diﬀerent colors). The result is
approximately a quadratically varying runtime. Likely worse for very low resolutions, since
this produces a large stride in the RF data (which is still of the same size as before), reducing
the cache eﬃciency. And for high-pixel-count output, it executes slightly faster than expected,
likely because this means there is only a tiny stride in the input RF data.
0
0.5

1.5

Figure 2.17: Comparison of time of execution with the B = 4 kernel, producing
an LRI with diﬀerent resolutions compared to 1871 × 337, but same geometry.
The region with very low resolution (or equivalently a large stride in RF data), is not that
relevant in practice. If this was the case, one might lower the RF sampling rate.
==PAGE:48==
30
Chapter 2. Beamformer
2.5
Discussion
Other optimizations
The main issue is the sample loading, and subsequent stalling. The path from L2 directly
to shared memory in ﬁg. 2.14 represent the new feature from compute capability 8.6, where
asynchronous data transfers can happen from global memory to shared memory, skipping
registers and the L1 cache. Essentially the use case is that data transfers can happen in a
pipelined manner, without costing registers. This was attempted in an early iteration, where
the cost appeared too high to give a speedup. But perhaps it could be used eﬃciently.
Warp-level communication was attempted for a cooperative loading of data samples, mean-
ing four threads cooperatively loaded the four consecutive samples needed for one of their
interpolations. The time-cost for communication was too high to give a speedup.
One could also look into using the texture units of the GPU to perform the linear inter-
polations needed for the apodizations. This was attempted at the early stages, but it did not
appear to give a speed-up at the time. These likely have a low throughput, as there are few
texture units6 (1 per scheduler) compared to FPUs (up to 32 per scheduler).
With the approaches discussed so far, a central issue appear to be a low throughput of
the speciﬁc instructions. This is likely also why the load instructions are so costly, with 27%
of time spent. Essentially speciﬁc units in the SM are oversubscribed, and the solution might
be to specialize, e.g. only one warp in the block to make use of the low-throughput unit.
Constant memory space can be used for the variables rx_apo_manual and rx_apo_lid in
the kernel for a speedup. This will stall immediately on a cache-miss, which can simply be
overlapped with the many load operations. Alternatively these variables could be packed
together with the rx_pos input, loading all three variables as a single pipelined float4.
Another alternative is to use more esoteric features, not exposed in CUDA C++. The
PTX ISA documents an instruction that allow for prefetching memory to a cache level:
prefetch.global.L2
[ptr];
// Compute capability >= 2.0 only
The keyword asm can be used to place inline PTX assembly inside CUDA code.
The main issue with any kind of tricks is that any instruction costs time, and the code is
already quite streamlined. Some strategy may be worth in the case B = 16, if its cost can be
amortized. Otherwise future work should look into software pipelining the sample loads, but
this can be diﬃcult to get right due to the branching on early continue.
Since beamforming conﬁguration is ﬁxed from sequence to sequence, a speedup will prob-
ably be given by hard-coding as many variables as possible into the kernel. This comes at
the cost of ﬂexibility, since changing the sequence will then require modifying the kernel.
Numerical diﬀerences
The use of --use_fast_math leads to to some functions being replaced with their single
precision intrinsic versions. The maximum error is documented in the PTX ISA [20], but
individual errors might vary between compute capabilities. There is also the option of replac-
ing them with versions with a certain documented maximum error over the ﬂoating point
range (-prec-div, -prec-sqrt ﬂags). For graphics, the diﬀerence is likely imperceptible.
Similarly, the use of atomicAdd indicates a non-deterministic order of summation, possibly
producing diﬀerent rounding errors between runs. This is just the nature of GPU computa-
tion, but can conﬂict with the principle of replicability of scientiﬁc research.
6These units appear to work best if warps use them in a broadcast pattern (all threads read the same
address), such that 1 warp can be serviced by the 1 Tex unit. Similar to using constant memory space.
==PAGE:49==
2.6. Conclusion
31
Load eﬃciency
Throughout the implementation, we did not investigate the eﬃciency of memory operations.
Cache eﬃciency can in many cases be increased by satisfying certain alignment and stride
requirements for the threads loading in the warp. The individual delays τ inside a warp lead
to indices being loaded without regard for an optimal memory access pattern.
The proﬁler gives warnings about the amount of over-fetching happening for the L1 and
L2 caches, but it is diﬃcult to improve upon without having to use another beamforming
algorithm. Any kind of computation/logic to improve the memory access pattern is likely
too costly, and may simply be replicating the functionality already present in the L1 cache.
For example, the four float2 samples to load are unaligned in memory. They could be
covered by three float4 load instructions, and sometimes just two. But the logic to do this
is more costly than any beneﬁt it will give. Using the L1 cache is just faster in our case, even
if there is some over-subscription of LD/ST units due to the many float2 requests.
2.6
Conclusion
It was demonstrated that real-time beamforming of the SURE sequences, at a rate of over
405 images per second was possible. The main bottleneck of the new micro-batched CUDA
beamformer are the many sample loading instructions that must be executed, followed by the
latency of individual memory accesses (like the previous algorithm [8]). A general method
for avoiding stall due to latencies, or a change in algorithm, would likely be needed to get a
further doubling of performance for this beamformer.
Figure 2.18: The equivalent beamforming rate with each iteration.

==PAGE:51==

Chapter 3
Motion estimation
3.1
Quadrature processing
3.1.1
Purpose
The purpose of the quadrature processing is to compute the analytic representations needed
for motion estimation. This will be elaborated upon in the Motion Estimation section later.
For transverse motion, a method known as transverse oscillation is used to extract signals
to estimate motion in the transverse direction and possibly the axial direction as well.
3.1.2
Theory
Operators
The analytic representation is computed by the operator A:
A r := F−1(a ⊙F r) = r + iHr
(3.1)
where ⊙is element-wise multiplication, F the Fourier transform, H is denoted the Hilbert
transform. a is an element that zeroes out the negative frequencies in the spectrum:
a(ξ) = 1 + sign ξ
(3.2)
1
2
a(ξ)
ξ
Figure 3.1: The element a(ξ).
Then the result is an element, A r, with a one-sided spectrum, which is useful for motion
estimation. For a discrete r ∈Cn (a vector), the deﬁnition of a depends on n . See [21] for a
deﬁnition, or the built-in MATLAB function hilbert for an implementation.
==PAGE:52==
34
Chapter 3. Motion estimation
Linearity
We need linearity. To show this, let x, y be some elements, and then:
A(x + y) = F−1(a ⊙F(x + y)) = F−1(a ⊙F x) + F−1(a ⊙F y) = A x + A y,
(3.3)
by the linearity of F and F−1 along with the distributive property of multiplication (⊙).
The Hilbert transform: Hr = (A r −r)/i is linear as well:
H(x + y) = A(x + y) −(x + y)
i
= A x −x
i
+ A y −y
i
= Hx + Hy,
(3.4)
where the linearity of A was used. These are all the tools that are needed for the theory.
Transverse oscillation
In this section we provide some simpliﬁcations to the transverse oscillation deﬁnitions from
[22]. We will denote Ax for the application in the transverse direction and Az for the applica-
tion in the axial – the only diﬀerence is on which axis the Fourier transform F is computed,
and the negative frequencies annihilated.
The calculation is then as follows [22] (paraphrased with the notation of this thesis):
rsq = Ax r
(3.5a)
rsqh = Hzr + iHzHxr
(3.5b)
r1 = rsq + irsqh
(3.5c)
r2 = rsq −irsqh
(3.5d)
rx = r1 ⊙r2
(3.5e)
Then rx can be used to estimate transverse motion. The ﬁrst simpliﬁcation is:
rsqh = Hz(r + iHxr) = Hz Ax r = Hzrsq,
(3.6)
using linearity. The computation of rsqh can be eliminated:
r1 = rsq + iHzrsq = Az rsq
(3.7)
And ﬁnally, r2 can be found simply from r1 + r2 = 2rsq ⇔r2 = 2rsq −r1. In summary:

(3.8)
with Ar := r −iHr = F−1((2 −a) ⊙F r)
(3.9)
The operator A performs an annihilation of positive frequencies. In general A r = Ar. The
A operators commute since multiplication (⊙) is commutative: Az Ax = Ax Az. One way to
see this is that e.g. Az Ax corresponds to multiplying the 2D spectrum with a(−ξz)a(ξx).
For the SURE pipeline, the analytic representation is done before beamforming, thus the
input to the function is rin = Az r. And r was originally real, s.t. rin = Az r = Azr:
r1 = Ax Az r = Ax rin
(3.10a)
r2 = Ax Azr = Ax rin
(3.10b)
Which eliminates the need to even compute rsq.
==PAGE:53==
3.1. Quadrature processing
35
Analytic Representation by Convolution
The Hilbert transform as well as analytic representation operator corresponds to a convolution
by some element. Due to the Fourier convolution theorem:
A = F−1(a ⊙F r) = (F−1 a) ∗r
(3.11)
where F−1 a is some element or generalized element.1 Note in the discrete case that the con-
volution would be a circular convolution, and so when a "same size" ﬁnite linear convolution
is applied, there will not necessarily be a total annihilation of negative frequencies.
What is done instead is that the analytic representation is applied to a matched ﬁlter.
And since convolutions commute, the result is practically the same as if A was applied after
matched ﬁltering. There are some considerations as to what the ﬁlter size should be. This
way analytic representation and ﬁltering can be done in one convolution operation.
Estimation of transverse frequency
In case the center frequency of a signal is not known, an unbiased estimate is given by [23]:
Frequency estimate =
R ∞
−∞ξP(ξ)dξ
R ∞
−∞P(ξ)dξ
(3.12)
Where P(ξ) :=

(1 + sign ξ) F r(ξ)
2 is the energy spectral density of A r.
1For functions it would be δ(t) + 1/(πt), which is a tempered distribution.

Explanation of the estimator
The value R(∆t) = Az rt+∆tAz rt is a complex autocorrelation at lag ∆t. For an actual signal,
this can be estimated empirically by, for example, averaging temporally or spatially (under
assumption of constant vz within this frame). Letting ϕ(t) = arg Az rt, we essentially have:
R(∆t) = ei(ϕ(t+∆t)−ϕ(t)) ≈eiϕ′(t)∆t ⇒arg R(∆t) ≈ϕ′(t) = 2π∆z

to the instantaneous frequency (in time) of the signal. For a non-monochromatic signal the
result would then be some scaling of the whole spectrum, and not just the one wavelength.

3.2.3
Considerations with the Estimators
How a transverse oscillation created is created
There are a few ways to introduce a transverse oscillation. For SURE imaging, it is currently
done by a ﬁltering. An illustration of the spectra (absolute value of 2D FFT) of a HRI:
0
x
0
z
0
x
0
Figure 3.2: The spectra. Note A includes the ﬁltering.
As can be seen in the ﬁrst spectrum in ﬁg. 3.2, there is not really a transverse "carrier" with
a well deﬁned center frequency. Instead an oscillation is introduced by designing a ﬁlter,
combining a cosine with a hanning window; then the analytic representation of the ﬁlter
is computed, and this is convolved with the image laterally to get Ax (with some abuse of
notation, as Ax is not just the analytic representation then, but includes ﬁltering.).
The wavelength of the cosine in the ﬁlter was made to match λz, but then the actual,
resulting wavelength, λx, is simply estimated. The reason only negative ξx-frequencies remain
in ﬁg. 3.2, appear to be because a conjugation of the Ax-transformed ﬁlter was performed.2
Why not just estimate transverse motion the same way as axial?
The issue is that a transverse motion estimator based on Ax r will be inﬂuenced by axial
motions due to the axial oscillation. The spectra on one frame is shown in ﬁg. 3.3
0
x

0
Figure 3.3: Spectra of diﬀerent ﬁltered signals. Arbitrary aspect ratio.
As can be seen from the ﬁrst spectrum, one might use rz to estimate axial motion as well.
Az r is currently used for SURE; it has a greater detection range, and the transverse ﬁltering
also blurs the image laterally slightly, so Az r may be more precise.
2This does not change anything with regards to the theory except the resulting sign on the motion estimate.
==PAGE:57==
3.2. Motion Estimators
39

Ax r = cos

Figure 3.4: Output of motion estimates between two frames. Arbitrary color axis.
But for the spectrum of rx, this oscillation is completely removed. transverse oscillations in
rx have a relatively high frequency compared to the axial sampling rate, which causes some
wrap-around of the spectrum.
In case of motion greater than detectable range
If any motion is greater than the detectable range of the motion estimators, there will be
aliasing. The phase will wrap around, and the result will be a motion estimate initially with
a high velocity, but in the opposite direction. SURE imaging uses a high frame-rate for a low
∆z and ∆x.
Improving the tissue motion estimates
In SURE imaging, a spatial averaging of the estimates is performed. This is because tissue
is relatively rigid, so there is a high spatial correlation in these values. The result is that an
estimator with a lower variance is produced.
High amplitude signals
Throughout the theory unit-amplitude signals were assumed. But when a spatial averaging
is done, the result is a circular mean where each phase-term eiϕ is weighted by what the
amplitude was at that point in the image. Or rather by essentially the squared amplitude for
axial estimator, and fourth-power of amplitude for the transverse motion estimator.

Chapter 3. Motion estimation
3.3
Implementation and Results
The implementations are also found in appendix B.2.
3.3.1
Quadrature Processing
The original implementation was a direct translation of the theory in eq. (3.5).
Listing 3.1: Previous computation of transverse oscillation
1 rsq = conv2(real(HRI_frame), lateral_pulse, 'same');
% Computes Ax r
2 hilbert_left=hilbert(real(rsq));
% Imaginary part of this is Hz r
3 hilbert_right=hilbert(imag(rsq)); % Imaginary part of this is HzHx r
4 rsqh=imag(hilbert_left)+sqrt(-1)*imag(hilbert_right);
5
6 r1 = rsq + sqrt(-1)*rsqh;
7 r2 = rsq - sqrt(-1)*rsqh;
The ﬁrst convolution is to perform both analytic representation as well as a ﬁltering. Calls
to hilbert each perform a FFT to compute Az (eq. (3.1)) – they compute A ℜand not H.
The rate was 639 HRI/s. The new implementation has a rate of around 15500 HRI/s:
Listing 3.2: With the simpliﬁcations.
1 function [r1, r2] = quadrature(HRI_frame, lateral_pulse)
2
% It is assumed the input HRI_frame is Az r - axial analytic representation.
3
% Essentially HRI_frame = conv2(HRI_frame, axial_pulse, 'same');
4
r1 = conv2(HRI_frame, lateral_pulse, 'same');
% Computes AzAx r
5
r2 = conv2(conj(HRI_frame), lateral_pulse, 'same');
6 end
The time was measured by running gputimeit(@()quadrature(HRI_frame, lateral_pulse)).
The inputs were a 1871 × 337 complex ﬂoat HRI image and a 1 × 13 complex ﬂoat ﬁlter.
Quadrature procssing rate for 1871x337 input.

3.3. Implementation and Results
41
3.3.2
Motion Estimation
The previous implementation of the axial motion estimator is straight-forward:
Listing 3.3: Previous axial motion estimate.
1
2 % Find the axial autocorrelation function for relative motion.
3 Rxx_rel = HRI_previous_frame .* conj(HRI_frame);
4
5 % Calculate a spatial mean using separable filters
6 Rxx_mean = conv2(conv2(Rxx_rel, filt_axial, 'same'), filt_lateral, 'same');
7
8 z_motion_rel_est = -lambda_z_est * atan2(imag(Rxx_mean), real(Rxx_mean))/(4*pi);
Which runs with a rate of 2848 Hz, with input images of size 1871 × 337, and ﬁlters of size
320 × 1 and 1 × 26. It can be made to run slightly faster (3015 Hz) by:
z_motion_rel_est = -lambda_z_est/(4*pi)*angle(Rxx_mean);
The transverse motion estimation was computed as in the literature [24]:
Listing 3.4: Previous transverse motion estimate.
1 % Calculate the lag at one autocorrelation
2 R1_rel = conj(r1_previous).*r1;
3 R2_rel = conj(r2_previous).*r2;
4
5 % Estimate the lateral velocity components
6 R12_nominator
= imag(R1_rel).*real(R2_rel) + real(R1_rel).*imag(R2_rel);
7 R12_denominator = real(R1_rel).*real(R2_rel) - imag(R1_rel).*imag(R2_rel);
8
9 R12_nominator_mean = conv2(conv2(R12_nominator, filt_axial, 'same'), filt_lateral
, 'same');
10 R12_denominator_mean = conv2(conv2(R12_denominator, filt_axial, 'same'),
filt_lateral, 'same');
11
12 x_motion_rel_est = -lambda_x_est*atan2(R12_nominator_mean, R12_denominator_mean)
/(4*pi);
Where the value R12_denominator is equal to the real part of R1_rel · R2_rel, and
R12_nominator is equal to the imaginary part of this. The rate was 1840 Hz. To reduce
the amount of scattered memory accesses, the code can be changed to:
1 % Compute lateral motion signal.
2 rx = r1 .* r2;
3 rx_previous = r1_previous .* r2_previous;
4
5 % Calculate the lateral motion estimate
6 R12 = rx .* conj(rx_previous); % = R1_rel * R2_rel
7 R12_mean = conv2(conv2(R12, filt_axial, 'same'), filt_lateral, 'same');
8 x_motion_rel_est = -lambda_x_est/(4*pi)*angle(R12_mean);
which gives a rate of 2643 Hz. One could compute and store just rx = r1 · r2 instead of
both r1 and r2. If this is done outside the function, the rate becomes 3003 Hz – essentially
this is the rate of line 6-8 above. Most time is spent with the convolutions.
This was the implementations of quadrature processing and motion estimation.
The
estimates are then used to motion-correct images such that they are aligned for stationary
echo subtraction, removing the tissue signal from the ultrasound image.
==PAGE:60==
42
Chapter 3. Motion estimation
MATLAB optimizes some commands
Out of interest, inspecting the SASS code for the execution of angle(x), it turns out surpris-
ingly to be the same kernel as atan2(imag(x), real(x)). Executing the latter expression,
taken literally, would launch three kernels; but it appears MATLAB does interpret some
commands in a way, so as not to under-perform3. Usually, the function arrayfun is used to
have MATLAB do a custom kernel fusion, it just appears to not be necessary in all cases.
Why the new axial motion code was faster
Even a scaled angle angle(x)*k computed in a single kernel launch. The only reason the
previous code for z_motion_rel_est was slower, was that pi is double precision:
% Just as fast. Twice as fast as the initial.
z_motion_rel_est = -lambda_z_est*angle(Rxx_mean)/(4*single(pi));
This is unexpected as a ﬂoat (’single’ type) times a double-precision ﬂoating point number
(’double’ type) result in a ﬂoat. As an example:
Listing 3.5: Example of "slow" MATLAB GPU code.
test = ones(1000, 1, 'single', 'gpuArray');
test * pi
% <- The result is single precision.
This results in a single kernel, with intermediates computed in double-precision:
Listing 3.6: Subset of SASS code for the slow case.
#
Instruction
Interpretation
12
LDG.E R2, [R2.64]
Load value in test array
14
F2F.F64.F32 R4, R2
Convert value to double
15
DMUL R4, R4, c[0x0][0x170]
Multiply value with pi
16
F2F.F32.F64 R5, R4
Convert back to single
17
STG.E [R6.64], R5
Write result
18
EXIT
And the reason the new code was faster was simply because of precedence rules. Literals such
as 4 are double, but the variable lambda_x_est was single precision, which coerced pi to single.
The following results in just one CUDA kernel with only single precision intermediates.
Listing 3.7: Example of "fast" MATLAB GPU code.
% The following results in one CUDA kernel with only single intermediates:
x = test * (pi*single(1));
y = pi * single(1) * test;
z = single(1) * pi * test;
% If the result is not stored/printed, zero kernels are launched.
3This cannot be done in general due to the full employment theorem for compiler writers (Rice’s theorem).
It is probably the just-in-time compilation feature of the MATLAB execution engine.
==PAGE:61==
43
Chapter 4
Spline Interpolation
4.1
Introduction
For stationary echo subtraction, the images need to be aligned using the estimated motion.
Spline interpolation was found to be superior to Lagrange interpolation, but it is more costly
to compute, and therefore an eﬃcient implementation is needed.
The latest MATLAB version (R2022a) supports only 1D spline interpolation on the GPU.
Reverse Engineering MATLAB
The MATLAB implementations of interp1 and interp2 are closed-source and proprietary,
similar to other functions that are designed for high performance (e.g. mtimes). The underly-
ing implementation may be C++ or Fortran. But it turned out that the MATLAB built-in
function spline had a MATLAB implementation, which could be viewed with the command:
edit spline
The initial implementations were developed based on reverse engineering this functionality.
The function csapi from the Curve Fitting Toolbox, which supports multidimensional splines,
as well as other features, gave inspiration on how to perform 2D spline interpolation. These
two functions compute the spline pieces in monomial form, and have a large memory footprint.
The process was as follows:
1. Hypothesize interp1 is similar to spline.
2. Verify their outputs are the close within rounding precision.
3. Simplify spline to reduce memory footprint using another polynomial form.
4. Implement gpuArray.arrayfun spline evaluation.
5. Expand to 2D interpolation referencing the Wikipedia page on Bicubic Interpolation
[25], along with csapi, and generalizing the new polynomial form.
6. Verify 2D version matches interp2 within rounding precision.
7. Implement spline evaluation in CUDA.
8. Investigate algorithms to construct the splines more eﬃciently (Tridiagonal solvers).
9. Fast 2D spline interpolation implementation was completed.
10. Develop theory to prove correctness using the Wikipedia page on Spline Interpolation
[26], and formalizing what the code does (many improvements were guesses initially).
==PAGE:62==
44
Chapter 4. Spline Interpolation
4.2
Theory
Spline interpolation involves joining polynomials of low degree, usually 3, to create an inter-
polating function. By using a low degree, Runge’s phenomenon is avoided.
4.2.1
Interpolation
The idea is that given a dataset of points representing RF data, possibly complex:
(xi, fi) ∈R × C,
i = 1 . . . n
(4.1)
we want to approximate the underlying function fi = f(xi) of the data. Informed by this
interpretation we can assume, without loss of generality1 (to simplify derivations):
x1 < x2 < . . . < xn
(4.2)
An interpolating function, S(x) : [x1, xn] →C, is one which satisﬁes
S(xi) = fi
(4.3)
In short form: S(x) = f.
4.2.2
Cubic polynomials
Monomial Form
A cubic polynomial q : R →C can be written on the monomial form, with coeﬃcients a ∈C4:

Thomas Algorithm
One might compute k by ﬁrst computing g = Pf, which can be done eﬃciently as a convo-
lution (then correcting the end-elements). Finally, solving Ck = g for k. This tridiagonal
system can be solved eﬃciently with the Thomas algorithm in O(n) time, instead of the O(n2)
time typical of a matrix-vector solver; but the algorithm is inherently sequential and therefore
not easily adapted for GPU processing. This will be discussed further in later sections.

4.2. Theory

4.2.5
Bicubic Hermite form
The bicubic Hermite form is the 2D generalization of the (1D) cubic Hermite form, and it
can be derived with the exact same approach, which will lead to a large 16 × 16 system of
equations. But there exists a more concise form: The 2D analogue to a monomial form is
q(s, t) = p(s)Ap(t)⊤
(4.23)
Where A ∈C4×4 are its polynomial coeﬃcients. The coeﬃcients are given by:
A = MVM⊤
(4.24)
Where V ∈C4×4 is a matrix of values and derivatives. Speciﬁcally, the result is:


q(0, 0)
q(0, 1)
q′
t(0, 0)
q′
t(0, 1)
q(1, 0)
q(1, 1)
q′
t(1, 0)
q′
t(1, 1)
q′
s(0, 0)
q′
s(0, 1)
q′′(0, 0)
q′′(0, 1)
q′
s(1, 0)
q′
s(1, 1)
q′′(1, 0)
q′′(1, 1)

= V
(4.25)
And this can be seen to hold by inserting t, s ∈{0, 1} into the equations:
q(s, t) := p(s)MVM⊤p(t)⊤
(4.26a)
q′
s(s, t) := ∂q
∂s(s, t) = p(s)DMVM⊤p(t)⊤
(4.26b)
q′
t(s, t) := ∂q
∂t (s, t) = p(s)MVM⊤D⊤p(t)⊤
(4.26c)
q′′(s, t) := ∂2q
∂s∂t(s, t) = p(s)DMVM⊤D⊤p(t)⊤
(4.26d)
E.g. q(0, 0) = v11, q′′(1, 1) = v44. And eq. (4.26a) is the bicubic Hermite form.
Space eﬃciency
There is a massive usefulness in using the bicubic Hermite form (eq. (4.26a)), over the form in
eq. (4.23) since it lowers the memory required. A spline over Nx × Ny points require a space
of 16(Nx −1)(Ny −1) to store the coeﬃcients A, but only 4NxNy space is needed to store
the per-point values and derivatives V, essentially since these are shared between points.
In fact, further compression is even possible for C2 spline interpolation by eq. (4.18):

ki+1


(4.27)
This system will then halve the memory requirement for the 1D derivatives k.
In a D-dimensional spline interpolation, the storage requirement is per point is O(4D) for
coeﬃcients, O(2D) for values and derivatives, and the lower bound is O(1) for just the values.
==PAGE:68==
50
Chapter 4. Spline Interpolation
4.2.6
Bicubic Spline Interpolation
This is the 2D version of spline interpolation. In this case we have points
(pij, fij) ∈R2 × C,
for i = 1 . . . Nx, j = 1 . . . Nz
(4.28)
Now pij = (xij, zij) are 2D coordinates. Again, we assume monotonicity on this grid:
x1j < x2k < · · · < xNxj
for j = 1 . . . Nz
zi1 < zi2 < · · · < ziNz
for i = 1 . . . Nx
(4.29)
And we assume this grid is rectilinear2. The interpolating spline is a piecewise polynomial:
S : [x11, xNxNz] × [z11, zNxNz] →C
(4.30)
consisting of (Nx −1)(Nz −1) pieces, qij : [xij, zij] × [xi+1,j+1, zi+1,j+1] →C, such that
S(p) = qij(p) for p in the domain of qij (valid, since the spline is required to be continuous).
The idea is completely analogous to the 1D spline, and the same approach could be used
to ﬁnd a massive sparse system of equations. A more concise form exists, which is given now.
Bicubic Spline Interpolation – Construction
Apply the 1D spline routine (eq. (4.21)) to get the spline derivatives:
K := SF
(4.31a)
K′ := FS⊤
(4.31b)
K′′ := SFS⊤
(4.31c)
Where F = (fij) ∈CNx×Nz is the input image. Note the spline derivative systems S must
match the size of the dimension it is multiplied onto. This means Nx, Nz ≥4 is a requirement.
Finally, set:
Vij :=


fij
fi,j+1
k′
ij
k′
i,j+1
fi+1,j
fi+1,j+1
k′
i+1,j
k′
i+1,j+1
kij
ki,j+1
k′′
ij
k′′
i,j+1
ki+1,j
ki+1,j+1
k′′
i+1,j
k′′
i+1,j+1


(4.32)
And let these be the values and derivatives for the respective pieces qij(s, t) expressed in
bicubic Hermite form, with
s =
x −xij
xij −xi+1,j+1
∧t =
z −zij
zij −zi+1,j+1
(4.33)
It is not immediately obvious which properties are satisﬁed by this 2D spline, other than the
interpolative property. Continuity requires the marginal functions match, and not just end
points. We will therefore prove the C2 property, as this is central to spline interpolation.
2It is possible to generalize to convex grid cells.
==PAGE:69==
4.2. Theory
51
4.2.7
Proof of Bicubic Spline Properties
This section serves as a proof of properties with the construction given.
The deﬁnition of S ∈Ck is that for any 0 ≤d1, d2, satisfying d1 +d2 = d ≤k, the derivative:
∂dS
∂d1x∂d2z
(4.34)
exists and is continuous. And continuity for the spline simply means the pieces match on the
margins. From the 1D cubic Hermite form, it can be seen that the cubics are equal iﬀ. they
agree on value and derivative at the end-points; this fact gives us an easier basis to work in.
The system S is not needed till the very end, since it enforces S ∈C2. Tedious details are left
out, meaning most proofs here are in sketch form.
Continuity
Consider qij(s, 1) and qi,j+1(s, 0) as 1D Hermite forms. Note their domain overlap – they lie
on top of each other. And since they agree on value and derivative on the end-points:
qij(0, 1) = (Vij)12 = fi,j+1 = qi,j+1(0, 0)
(4.35a)
qij(1, 1) = (Vij)22 = fi+1,j+1 = qi,j+1(1, 0)
(4.35b)
q′
ij(0, 1) = (Vij)32 = ki,j+1 = q′
i,j+1(0, 0)
(4.35c)
q′
ij(1, 1) = (Vij)42 = ki+1,j+1 = q′
i,j+1(1, 0)
(4.35d)
they are equal. (use eq. (4.25) and eq. (4.32)). Notice the left-hand sides are simply column
2 in Vij, and the right-hand sides, column 1 in Vi,j+1. Similar for the other axis, one can
show: qij(1, t) = qi+1,j(0, t), which means S ∈C0.
Second order smoothness
In this case, one can verify eq. (4.34) for d1 + d2 = d ≤2 by:
∂dqij
∂d1s∂d2t(s, 1) = ∂dqi,j+1
∂d1s∂d2t(s, 0)
(4.36)
Essentially the same approach as for continuity (zeroth order smoothness), except the poly-
nomials are not considered 1D forms when the partial derivative is taken. Equivalently:
p(s)Dd1MVijM⊤(Dd2)⊤p(1)⊤= p(s)Dd1MVi,j+1M⊤(Dd2)⊤p(0)⊤
(4.37)
And of course a similar condition must hold on the other axis. Most cases are easily veriﬁed,
as they do not rely on the properties of K, K′ or K′′; therefore we omit these.
There are just two diﬃcult cases:
p(s)MVijM⊤(D2)⊤p(1)⊤= p(s)MVi,j+1M⊤(D2)⊤p(0)⊤
(4.38a)
p(1)D2MVijM⊤p(t)⊤= p(0)D2MVi+1,jM⊤p(t)⊤
(4.38b)
==PAGE:70==
52
Chapter 4. Spline Interpolation
We will show just the ﬁrst case, eq. (4.38a), as the second case follows similarly. Deﬁne:
lhs(s) := p(s)MVijM⊤(D2)⊤p(1)⊤= ∂qi,j
∂t2 (s, 1) = p(s)MVij
