(4.42)
And this system should be immediately recognizable as the conditions for C2 smoothness
(eq. (4.18)) of 1D splines, prescribing that K′ is given by applying a 1D spline derivative
system on the second axis: K′ = FS⊤, and that K′′ = KS⊤.
The stricly non-circular argument is: K′ and K′′ satisfy eq. (4.42) by the deﬁnition of S.
Similar conditions can be shown to hold for eq. (4.38b) with K and K′′ . Therefore S ∈C2.
==PAGE:71==
4.3. Implementation
53
4.3
Implementation
4.3.1
Spline derivative solvers
MATLAB internal tridiagonal solver
MATLAB R2021b introduced GPU support for the built-in spline, which uses an internal,
undocumented, tridiagonal system solver for gpuArrays. Found using the command:
edit gpuArray.spline
Initially this solver was used, but it had an unsatisfactory performance.
cuSPARSE tridiagonal solvers
The CUDA cuSPARSE library [29] API includes a few tridiagonal solver functions:
Listing 4.1: The cuSPARSE tridiagonal solvers
// Replace '<t>' with 'S' for real float input, 'C' for complex float input
cusparse<t>gtsv2()
// Uses pivoting for numerical stability
cusparse<t>gtsv2_nopivot()
// Without pivoting, for performance
cusparse<t>gtsv2StridedBatch()
// Batched, without pivoting
cusparse<t>gtsvInterleavedBatch()
A quick MEX implementation was made, calling gtsv2_nopivot, but the performance was
unsatisfactory. The use of the cuSPARSE API was therefore abandoned at an early stage.
Additionally, the two batched versions are made for solving multiple systems:
Ciki = ˆki
(4.43)
for ki, i = 1 . . . B. In our case we just needed to solve one system with many right-hand-sides:
Cki = ˆki = Pfi
(4.44)
An implementation was made to exploit this fact. Discussed in the next section.
cuThomasConstantBatch-based solver
An implementation was made based on cuThomasConstantBatch [30], which is derived from
cuThomasBatch [31], but specialized for a single constant system. cuThomas(Batch) is one
of the options in gtsvInterleavedBatch, and it is based on the Thomas algorithm.
The Thomas algorithm consists of an LU decomposition of the tridiagonal matrix, followed
by a forward sweep and backward substitution to get a solution. In cuThomasConstantBatch,
the LU decomposition would be pre-computed, and the kernel then simply perform the for-
ward and backward iterations over the data in a straight-forward manner.
As discussed in the theory, this is an inherently sequential process. But many uniform
sequential processes can be batched and run in parallel, which is what these algorithms do.
Each thread is made to run an instance of the Thomas algorithm, and care must be taken
for eﬃcient memory access. The input image is:
h
f1
f2
· · ·
fNz
i
=


(f1)1
(f2)1
· · ·
(fNx)1
(f1)2
(f2)2
· · ·
(fNx)2
...
...
...
...
(f1)Nz
(f2)Nz
· · ·
(fNx)Nz


= F ∈CNz×Nx
(4.45)
==PAGE:72==
54
Chapter 4. Spline Interpolation
where the leading (contiguous) dimension is of size Nz. But this means all the ﬁrst elements
in the forward sweep have a large stride of Nx between them. Therefore "interleaving" must
be performed, transposing F, such that all the ﬁrst elements are next to each other in memory,
and similar for the second elements, and so on. Only then can warps eﬃciently load the data.
In other words, cuThomas-based algorithms cannot run eﬃciently on the leading dimension
(which should have a size of 32 or greater), but only on the dimensions that follow.
Using a pre-solved system
A simple solution in MATLAB to computing the 1D spline derivatives would be:
% Given an input vector f, compute the derivatives for its spline interpolation.
k = C\(P*f);
% \ solves the system using Gaussian Elimination
Note that matrix-vector multiplication is a naturally batching operation, in the sense:
h
Pf1
Pf2
· · ·
Pfn
i
= P
h
f1
f2
· · ·
fn
i
= PF
(4.46)
Likewise for linear system solving, and therefore loops are not needed for a batched imple-
mentation. Given an input image F, the derivatives can found simply as:
K = C\(P*F)
Which solves
CK = PF
(4.47)
for K. But this is still sub-optimal – two passes over the data are required, one to get tmp=C*F,
and another for K=C\tmp. An alternative is:
S = C\P;
% Computes C^(-1)*P (do this in double-precision, then cast to single)
K = S*F;
Now this will initially be slower since computing S = C−1P is costly. But in our case the
system is the same from image to image, and therefore the resulting S can be memoized, and
re-used for every image. Matrix multiplication is an embarrassingly parallel operation, very
well-suited for a GPU. Highly optimized implementations exists in the cuBLAS library [32],
which is what MATLAB makes use of.3 It would be diﬃcult to beat this implementation for
a small system, such as the S ∈R337×337 multiplied onto the right side of F.
The numerical properties of this method can be analyzed from S. As discussed in sec-
tion 4.2.4, the oﬀ-diagonal elements of this matrix decay rapidly; but its condition number is
quite large, with the L2-norm based condition number being 133249584 for S ∈R1871×1871.
Eﬃcient mixed complexity matrix multiplication
In case of complex input, the cuBLAS GEMM functions need both S and F to be complex.
It was found to be faster to compute the derivatives using real matrix multiplication:
1 % Compute K=S*F using K=complex(S*real(F), S*imag(F)).
2 rK = S*real(F);
3 iK = S*imag(F);
Then the computed derivatives can be restored to complex form by K=complex(rK, iK), which
would lead to a faster spline evaluation kernel due to less scattered memory. But in our case
the spline is evaluated only once, and so it was not time-eﬃcient to do this interleaving.
3Revealed by kernel tracing with NVIDIA Nsight Systems.
==PAGE:73==
4.3. Implementation
55
Spline derivatives by convolution
The approximation by using convolution the from section 4.2.4, results in a highly eﬃcient
implementation. The built-in MATLAB convolution functions support gpuArrays.
The ﬁlter s may be created by:
k = 16; % +1 to account for the 'valid' convolution below removing one.
cinv = sqrt(3)/6*(-2+sqrt(3)).^abs( -(k+1):(k+1) );
s = conv(cinv, [3, 0, -3], 'valid')';
% A size 1+2*16 filter.
It can just be computed once and memoized. With regards to precision, it can be computed
in double-precision and rounded to single at the last stage. One beneﬁt with a FIR ﬁlter such
as this is that it is numerically stable. The output will be no greater than
k
X
i=−k
s(i)
 <
∞
X
i=−∞
s(i)
 ≤6
∞
X
i=−∞
c−1
i
 = 6 · 3 −
√
3
√
3 −1 ≈10.39
(4.48)
times the largest value in the input. The ﬁlter width k can be adjusted to reduce the amount
of approximation error. Since −2+
√
3 ≈−0.268, each added coeﬃcient to the ﬁlter is about
quarter the magnitude from the previous, meaning not too many coeﬃcients are needed
before the multipliers are eﬀectively zero within the range of inputs in SURE imaging.
A quick test determined that for k = 80, the last coeﬃcient (calculated in double precision)
is exactly zero within single precision. A convolution with a ﬁlter of size 2 · 79 + 1 = 159 is
usually faster than a matrix multiplication by some, e.g. 1871 × 1871, matrix.
Listing 4.2: Example of spline derivatives by convolution.
% Given an F, compute the derivatives for spline interpolation.
Kz = conv2(F, s, 'same');
% Axial derivatives
Kx = conv2(F, s', 'same');
% Lateral derivatives
Kb = conv2(Kx, s, 'same');
% Mixed derivatives
There will be some error on these edges. What one can do is for example to multiply F by
only the subset of S which corresponds to the edges, e.g. 79 × 1871. Some of this subset may
be zero within ﬂoat precision, such that it is suﬃcient to multiply only a square or rectangular
patch in F of size e.g. 159 × 79 with a 79 × 159 subset of S to eliminate the edge error.
This algorithm will probably be hard to beat in terms of performance since highly opti-
mized convolution and matrix multiplication implementations exists.
4.3.2
Spline evaluation
The spline evaluation code was made as a kernel which takes the matrix F ∈CNz×Nx, as well as
the six arrays making out the real and complex parts of the derivatives. It was made to allow
for N-dimensional inputs, such that a single kernel launch can do batched 2D interpolations
of some given tensor containing many images along with their points-to-evaluate-on.
This was one of the ﬁrst kernels that were implemented for this thesis.
It showed a
satisfactory performance early, so not much time was spent on it. The bicubic Hermite form
(eq. (4.26a)) is evaluated using Horner’s method – since the resulting form contains some
common sub-expressions of s2 and t2, these were calculated once and re-used the kernel.
==PAGE:74==
56
Chapter 4. Spline Interpolation
4.4
Results
Due to the way spline evaluation ends up reading scattered memory given the motion data,
the timings are dependent on the data and may vary from frame to frame. It is expected to
be faster if there is less motion. 31 coeﬃcients were used in the convolution ﬁlter s.
4.4.1
Performance
Motion and image data arbitrary frames were used: #21 to #24. Using a batched interpola-
tion on these four frames containing 8 HRIs, the average runtime of the spline construction
and evaluation was 4.3 msec, or 547 usec per HRI. This was over 500 repeats. See ﬁg. 4.5.

Chapter 5
Peak detection
5.1
Purpose
After the motion estimation has been completed, the stationary tissue is removed from the
image. First the images are motion corrected with the spline interpolation, such that the
tissue signals are expected to be aligned pixel-wise between frames. The alignment corrects
for tissue motion due to heartbeat, breathing, and other motion. Then if images are summed,
one expects tissue signals to add constructively. De-correlation is observed over longer time
periods, and the best result is currently given by simply using only neighbouring frames.
For each frame, the pixel-wise average of neighbouring frames provide an estimate of the
stationary tissue. This is then subtracted from the frame in question to produce an image of
the ﬂow. Peak detection is then performed on this image after log-compression.
Peaks are deﬁned as pixels where the value is greater than the neighbour values after this
processing. Some additional processing and thresholding is applied to better reject spurious
peaks, but this is implemented just using built-in MATLAB functions. The work done here
was to implement the peak-detection as well as sub-pixel peak localization on the GPU.
5.2
Theory
The peak detection uses a simple quadratic model:
f(x) = ax2 + bx + c
(5.1)
A peak is where the value in a pixel is greater than the neighbouring values. A model can
then be made from these three values, e.g. with f(−1), f(0) and f(1), where f(−1) < f(0)
and f(0) > f(1):


f(−1)
f(0)
f(1)

=


a −b + c
c
a + b + c

⇒
f(−1) −f(1)
2(f(−1) + f(1) −2f(0)) = −2b
2(2a) = −b
2a
(5.2)
This is then the equation that is used. It can be computed element-wise in the 2D case, and
if a pixel is at some position ̸= 0, the equation can still be used, but the oﬀset position must
be added afterwards.
Lastly, for the implementation, peaks with an estimated position that is further than a
radius of 0.5 pixels away are rejected. This makes sense, as then the sub-pixel peak position
is essentially close to another pixel than the one that was found to be a peak.
==PAGE:80==
62
Chapter 5. Peak detection
5.3
Implementation
The implementation is as follows:
1 #define READ2D(dx,dy) img[id.x+(dx) + (id.y+(dy))*xs]
2 #define CRIT2D(dx,dy) (value > READ2D((dx), (dy)))
3
4 __global__
5 void getPeaks2D(float * __restrict__ out, int * n_peaks,
6
const float * __restrict__ img, const int xs, const int ys,
7
int edge, const float threshold) {
8
edge = max(edge, 1);
// No access violation if supplied edge is 0.
9
for (int j = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y+edge; j < ys-edge;
10
j += blockDim.y * gridDim.y)
11
for (int i = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x+edge; i < xs-edge;
12
i += blockDim.x * gridDim.x) {
13
int2 id = make_int2(i, j);
14
float value = READ2D(0, 0);
15
bool isPeak = value > threshold &&
16
// Last check is if we are local maximum.
17
CRIT2D(-1, -1) && CRIT2D(0, -1) && CRIT2D(1, -1) &&
18
CRIT2D(-1,
0) && true
&& CRIT2D(1,
0) &&
19
CRIT2D(-1,
1) && CRIT2D(0,
1) && CRIT2D(1,
1);
20
21
if(!isPeak)
22
continue;
23
24
float2 dtop = make_float2(READ2D(-1, 0), READ2D(0, -1)) - make_float2(
READ2D(1, 0), READ2D(0, 1));
25
float2 dbot = make_float2(READ2D(-1, 0), READ2D(0, -1)) + make_float2(
READ2D(1, 0), READ2D(0, 1)) - 2.0f*value;
26
27
//Ensure that the interpolation is less then 0.5 as it else
28
// is a poor location, probably with multiple scatteres within
29
// the resolution cell
30
float2 dpos = dtop / (2.0f*dbot);
31
if(dpos.x*dpos.x + dpos.y*dpos.y <= 0.5f) {
32
int out_atomic = atomicAdd(n_peaks, 1);
33
reinterpret_cast<float2*>(out)[out_atomic] = make_float2(id.x, id.y)
34
+ dpos + 1.0f;
35
}
36
}
37 }
It is a straight-forward implementation with a grid-stride loop [33]. Grid-stride loops are
quite general, as they can be used in a conventional conﬁguration such as:
1 % n_samples_per_line = Nz = 1871
2 % n_lines = Nx = 337
3 kernel.ThreadBlockSize = [128 1];
4 kernel.GridSize = [ceil(n_samples_per_line/128) ceil(n_lines/1)];
Such that one grid spans the whole input, and no iterations of the loops are made. But with
this ﬂexible pattern, some experimentation found the following to perform better:
Listing 5.1: Kernel launch conﬁguration for peak detection.
1 D = gpuDevice;
2 peak_kernel.ThreadBlockSize = [256 1];
3 peak_kernel.GridSize = [D.MultiprocessorCount 16];
==PAGE:81==
5.3. Implementation
63
The main idea is that for short-running kernels, the overhead of starting blocks (e.g. com-
puting the variable id in the kernel) begin to become signiﬁcant. So instead of each thread
computing just one pixel (which might not even be a peak), a ﬁxed number of blocks are
created, and threads get to compute more than one value of the output.
Using a multiple of 82 blocks, each SM gets a block, but more than that is needed for
suﬃcient occupancy. Not much more thought went into it than that, other than just trying
diﬀerent sizes. A satisfying performance was quickly reached with the grid shown in listing
5.1. This is the utility of the grid-stride loop pattern. It generalizes most grids one would
initially think of, and a decent solution can often be found without much development time.
AtomicAdd for conﬂict-free writing
The peak positions must be written into a [2, N] array, where N is the number of peak
positions. The value N is not known in advance, and it is not time-eﬃcient to do two passes
just to count peaks before allocating memory to write them into. Instead an array of zeroes
of size [2, NxNz] is just given to the kernel. The kernel is essentially executed as:
1 peak_kernel = get_kernel('getPeaks2D');
2 peakpos = zeros(2, numel(image),'single','gpuArray');
3 [peakpos, count] = feval(peak_kernel, peakpos, 0,
4
image, size(image, 1), size(image, 2), edge, threshold);
5 peakpos = peakpos(:, 1:count);
The number of peaks N is initialized as zero, and a pointer to this number is given to each
thread in the kernel. The function atomicAdd is used to increment this counter without race-
conditions. atomicAdd returns the previous value, which shows where a peak position can be
written in the peak-position array.
For example, the ﬁrst thread to write a peak reads 0 and increments N to 1. It then
writes its peak position into the zeroth position of the peak position array. The result is a
non-deterministic ordering of the peaks in the peak-position array, which is ﬁne. Then after
the kernel has run, the peak-position array can be turned into the size that was needed.
Peak accumulation
A function was also made to accumulate the peaks into the image. This is only used for
low-resolution images, and not the pictures which are shown in this report. In this case each
thread just reads a value from the peak-position array and uses atomicAdd to increment the
value at the pixel corresponding to this position.
Implementations are found in appendix D.2.

The loop was placed inside the MEX function, but in real-time use it should be part of the
MATLAB code outside. With the use of the Streams API and asynchronous execution this
would take considerably more time to implement correctly, so it was not done for time.
This is followed by estimation of λx and λz:
Listing 6.2: Frequency Estimation
1 % Filter was created based on expected lambda_x, not the estimated.
2 lateral_pulse = cos(2*pi/lambda_x*(0:dx:M_TO*lambda_x));
3 lateral_pulse = hilbert( lateral_pulse .* hanning(length(lateral_pulse)).' );
4
5 % Estimate frequencies based on the first frame.
6 HRI_frame = HRIs(:,:, 1, 1);
7 rsq = conv2(real(HRI_frame), lateral_pulse, 'same');
8 lambda_x_est = abs(1/estimate_mean_frequency(rsq', dx));
9 lambda_z_est = abs(1/estimate_mean_frequency(HRI_frame, dz))*2;
10
11 % Original axial wavelength was 0.1540 mm, estimated wavelength is 0.1655 mm
12 % Filter lateral wavelength was 0.3080 mm, estimated wavelength is 0.3602 mm
13 % Transmit was set to frequency 10 MHz, but observed frequency is 9.3065 MHz
14
15 % Finally, filt_axial and filt_lateral are computed based on the lambdas:
16 % filt_axial = tukeywin(... lambda_z_est ..., 0.5); % Omitted for brevity.
These values are used for the motion estimation, and also determine the size of the axial and
lateral spatial averaging ﬁlters filt_axial and filt_lateral used in motion estimation.

This is followed by a loop performing motion correction

1 % Motion correction and echo subtraction time: 0.15264 rate: 628.9391
2 [x_index, z_index] = meshgrid(1:size(HRI, 2), 1:size(HRI, 1));
3 for frame=1:size(HRIs, 4)

8

% Size is [1871, 337, 1]

lambda_z_est, lambda_x_est);
12

14

15

16

18

19

20

23
24
% Motion correction of HRIs.
25

26
broadcast(x_region), broadcast(z_region), 'spline');
27
28
% The frame is needed for the next iteration of motion estimation.
29
rx_previous = rx
30
HRI_frame_previous = HRI_frame;
31 end
32
33 % Temporal filter.
34 filter_around = [-0.5, 1, -0.5];
35 filter_around = reshape(filter_around, 1, 1, 1, []);
% [1, 1, 1, 3]
36
37 % Apply stationary echo cancelling.
38 blood_flow_images = convn(HRIs, filter_around, 'valid');
39 blood_flow_images = 20*log10(abs(blood_flow_images));
% B-mode log-compression.
40
41 % The reference 0 dB is based on the first frame.
42 scale_factor = max(proc_data(:, :, 1), [], 'all')-dB_range;
% dB_range = 60.
43 blood_flow_images = blood_flow_images - scale_factor;
The rate for motion correction, with a batching of two frames (4 HRIs) is around 600 HRIs/s.
The MATLAB proﬁler shows around 50% of time is spent in interp2gpu and 25% in
motion_estimate. With approximated spline derivatives, the rate is around 725 HRIs/s.
With only one frame batches (2 HRIs), the rate is is much lower, at around 430 HRIs/s, and
80% of time is spent in interp2gpu, and 530 HRIs/s when using the spline approximation.
The echo subtraction, log-compression, and peak detection would in a real-time setting
be part of this loop. This was also implemented, and the performance of this is given in the
ﬁgures on the next page. See appendix B.1 for both implementations.

And with this we get a SURE pipeline rate of around 213 HRIs/s. The ultimate rate is
essentially the geometric average of rates of each pipeline step. If the time of beamforming is
left out, the rate is around 500 HRIs/s. If every operation, except beamforming, is brought
into the main loop, the SURE processing rate is on average 245 HRIs/s, as shown in ﬁg. 6.1.

==PAGE:86==
68
Chapter 6. SURE pipeline
MATLAB has a feature, where a section of code can be selected, and then converted to a func-
tion with the right click menu. Time-measurement was done by converting the beamforming
and SURE processing loop parts of the script into a function, which was then measured with
gputimeit. This measurement was done 100 times to produce the plots.
% Return the number of detections, such that the whole SURE pipeline is run.
N_detections = SUREfunc(t_start_index, rf_data_buffer, n_samples_per_line,
n_lines, tx_pos, center_focus, rx_pos, tx_vol, rx_vol, tx_f, rx_f,
tx_cos_angle, tx_sin_angle, tx_apo, rx_apo, rx_apo_manual, rx_apo_lid,
rx_apo_manual_lid, weights, emis_no_gpu, proc_options, dx, dz, lambda_z,
gui_para, lambda_x, geometry, gpu, filter_around))
The reason the code runs slightly faster compared to earlier measurements is likely that the
JIT compilation (as was seen in section 3.3.2) can much more aggressively optimize a function.
Intermediate variables inside of function are not stored in the workspace, meaning MATLAB
is allowed to remove any computations that are not used to produce the function outputs.
Latency
Throughput is not everything. For real-time visual feedback there needs to be little delay. For
the beamforming we saw a rate of around/over 200 frames (400 HRIs) per second. Batches
consisted of two frames, and as could be seen in ﬁg. 2.11, the sequential delays due to H2D
transfer followed by beamforming was around 13 msec.
Two frames at a time were also
processed in batches for the spline interpolations in the SURE processing loop, and the
latency in this loop can be measured to be around 9 msec. The result is a delay over 20 msec.
More time could be spent on improving this. Remember two frames currently have to
be ready before the H2D transfers start, meaning there is some additional time to wait for
this. Memory transfers can be made at a higher granularity, for example transferring data
for 1 LRI at a time. And with a simpliﬁed beamformer, the delay-and-summing could also
be done one LRI at a time, which might bring the delay of this part closer to only one frame
(2 HRI), corresponding to 2/(400 Hz) = 5 msec.
6.2
Discussion
Throughout the project, the majority of focus has been on GPU implementation. But the
CPU could potentially be used to perform some early computations that would be diﬃcult to
perform eﬃciently in parallel. For example re-ordering data as it is received, before sending
it to the GPU. The beamforming step is currently the most costly. But it could potentially
be done by a dedicated device.
It has also not been quantiﬁed in-depth how well the algorithm performs on other GPU
devices, or on other datasets; meaning there is the possibility of overly optimistic performance.
But conversely, many parts are conﬁgurable, meaning it can potentially be tuned for the data,
but it is still a concern.
6.3
Conclusion
The goal of reaching a SURE image processing rate of over 400 images a second was quickly
reached as part of this thesis work.
A high performance GPU implementation of spline
interpolation was made, which brought with it a real-time performance.
Attention was turned to the beamformer that produces the input images, and a new GPU
beamformer implementation was made, capable of producing up to 400 images per second
using a synthetic aperture. The result was a pipeline that runs with an average rate of 245
images per second, bringing the project on 3D real-time SURE imaging closer to a reality.

The matrix representation of any linear map is found by applying it to the basis vectors1:
Listing A.1: Matlab code for how to ﬁnd the matrix representation of the map.
1 n = 4;
2 I = eye(n);
% Identity matrix of size n. Gives the canonical basis.
3
4 % Now define the linear map, in this case 'same' size convolution with p:
5 f = @(x) conv(x, [3; 0; -3], 'same');
6
7 % The matrix representation is given by applying the map on each basis vector.
8 for i = 1:n
9
F(:, i) = f(I(:, i));
10 end
11
12 disp(F);

(A.1)
It makes sense from the deﬁnition of a convolution, that p is the reversed version of values
in P, as a convolution of functions f and g is:
(f ∗g)(t) :=
Z ∞
−∞
f(τ)g(t −τ) dτ
(A.2)
Such that g is reversed, and centered on/shifted to some t (row #) in the summation.
Well, then the inverse of a "same size" convolution, if it exists, can be found simply by
inverting the matrix representation.

Appendix A. Spline derivatives kernel
It should be clear now, that C and P resemble convolutions, and C−1 might resemble the
"inverse convolution" with (1, 4, 1). Using the Fourier convolution theorem:
f ∗F c = F−1(F f ◦F c) ⇒c−1 := F−1
 1
F c

(A.3)
Where F is the discrete Fourier transform and F−1 is its inverse. ◦is elementwise multipli-
cation, and the reciprocal is also element-wise. The element c−1 is then the inverse in terms
of this circular convolution, and exists only if F c has no zero entries.
This is a loose description, for example c must match f in size for a valid operation. To
give a speciﬁc example, a circular convolution with p in MATLAB:
% The circular convolution with p for n=4.
f = @(x) ifft( fft(x) .* fft([0; -3; 0;3]) );
Note in the code, p is padded to ﬁt f, here 4, and circular shifted, such that the center
element of is put ﬁrst, the elements before are placed last. It has the matrix representation:
f ∗F p =


(A.4)
The values of p wrap around in the top and bottom; similar to the original P, we ignore this
edge eﬀect – it is irrelevant for n →∞. An argument is that if our convolutional kernel is
rapidly decaying, any kind of error on the edges wont propagate.
Keeping track of padding can be diﬃcult, so ignore this detail. Let
cn := (1, 4, 1, 0, . . . , 0) ∈Rn
(A.5)

For negative i, the function is not on the form used above, since then there are then two

It has the following setup code, which is run before. The majority is not written by me, but
by Jørgen Arendt Jensen. However, it was collected from diﬀerent ﬁles of a larger project
and many operations are simply hard-coded here such that the SURE code could be tested
without invoking the larger framework it will be integrated in.

B.2. Motion estimation
87
B.2
Motion estimation
Frequency estimation
Computes the numerical integral to estimate frequencies.
1 %
Estimate the mean frequency in the signal to determine the bias factor
2 %
for the motion estimator.
3 %
4 %
The mean frequency is determined for the first index and averaged over
5 %
the second data index
6 %
7 %
Input:
rf_data - Complex rf data
8 %
dsamp
- Spatial sampling interval
9 %
10 %
Based on an implementation by Jørgen Arendt Jensen.
11
12 function [fmean] = estimate_mean_frequency(rf_data, dsamp)
13 [Nd, M]=size(rf_data);
14
15 % Make N a power of two
16 N=power(2,ceil(log2(Nd)));
17 Nf=max([N 512]);
18 f=ifftshift((0:Nf-1)'/Nf-0.5);
% Instead of fftshift on rf_data.
19
20 %
Calculate the frequency spectra
21 P = abs(fft(
rf_data.*tukeywin(Nd, 0.2)
, Nf)).^2;
22
23 % Find the mean frequency from averaging
24 fmean = sum(P .* f, 1) ./ sum(P, 1); % Per row mean spatial frequency.
25 fmean = sum(fmean) ./ sum(fmean>0); % Just average over image. (It does decrease
a bit with distance for lambda_x it seems).
26 fmean = fmean/dsamp;
27 end
Motion estimation and Quadrature processing
1 function [r1, r2] = quadreture(HRI_frame, lateral_pulse)
2 % rsq = convn(real(HRI_frame), lateral_pulse, 'same');
% Computes Ax r
3 r1 = convn(HRI_frame, lateral_pulse, 'same');
% Computes AzAx r
4 r2 = convn(conj(HRI_frame), lateral_pulse, 'same'); % = 2 * rsq - r1;
5 % rmult = r1 .* (2 * rsq - r1);
6 end
1 function [z_motion_rel_est, x_motion_rel_est] = motion_estimate(HRI_frame,
HRI_previous_frame, rx, rx_previous, filt_axial, filt_lateral, lambda_z_est,
lambda_x_est)
2 % Find the axial autocorrelation function for relative motion.
3 Rxx_rel = HRI_previous_frame .* conj(HRI_frame);
4 % Calculate a spatial mean using separable filters
5 Rxx_mean = convn(convn(Rxx_rel, filt_axial, 'same'), filt_lateral, 'same');
6 z_motion_rel_est = -lambda_z_est * atan2(imag(Rxx_mean), real(Rxx_mean))/(4*
single(pi));
7
8 % Compute lateral motion signal.
9 % Calculate the lateral motion estimate
10 R12 = rx .* conj(rx_previous);
11 R12_mean = convn(convn(R12, filt_axial, 'same'), filt_lateral, 'same');
12 x_motion_rel_est = -lambda_x_est/(4*single(pi))*angle(R12_mean);
13 end

B.3
Implementation of Spline Interpolation
1 function Vq = interp2gpu(V, Xq, Yq, method)
2 %INTERP2GPU interpolation for 2-D gridded gpuArray data, based on interp2.
3 %
4 %
[Vq] = interp2gpu(V, Xq, Yq, method) is equivalent to
5 %
[Vq] = interp2(V, Xq, Yq, method, 0)
6 %
7 % This means
8 % 1) No extrapolation is used. Outside values are set to zero.
9 % 2) Assumes a unit-spaced grid: X=1:N and Y=1:M where [M,N]=SIZE(V, 1:2).
10 %
11 % It supports an additional method on GPU:
12 % [Vq] = interp2gpu(V, Xq, Yq, 'spline_approx') fast approximate spline.
13 %
14 % Batching is supported for spline interpolation! A GPU is high-throughput,
15 % so interpolating a [M,N,K]=SIZE(V, 1:3) video will be much faster than
16 % processing each frame individually.
17
18 % TODO: We do not support all the call signatures of interp2 (e.g. Xq, Yq
19 % vectors), and we should also verify that non-complex input works as well.
20 % Maybe in the furture: Add double precision float and CPU support.
21
22 if nargin < 4
23
method = 'linear';
24 end
25
26 if strcmp(method, 'nearest') || ...
27
strcmp(method, 'linear')
|| ...
28
strcmp(method, 'cubic')
|| ...
29
~isgpuarray(V)
30
% Cases where we can just fall back to built-in interp2.
31
Vq = interp2(V, Xq, Yq, method, 0);
32
return;
33 end
34 % Ensure gpuArray.
35 Xq = gpuArray(Xq);
36 Yq = gpuArray(Yq);
37
38 spline_approx = strcmp(method, 'spline_approx');
39
40 if strcmp(method, 'spline_slerp')
41
% For slerp, we spline interpolate the absolute value, and slerp (I.e.
42
% phase-linear) interpolate the phase. Means we only need to store half
43
% as much data for derivatives, thus approximately doubling speed.
44
derivatives = gpuThomas(abs(V));
45
interpolation2D = get_kernel('getInterpolation2D_special');
46
Vq = feval(interpolation2D, V, V, derivatives, Yq, Xq, size(V, 1), size(V, 2)
);
47
return;
48 elseif strcmp(method, 'spline') || spline_approx
49
derivatives = gpuThomas2D(V, spline_approx);

51

size(V, 2));

The function to compute the spline derivatives.
1 function derivatives = gpuThomas2D(V, approx_dim1)
2
%GPUTHOMAS2D Computes the derivatives for 2D spline interpolation.
3
%
4
% [derivatives] = GPUTHOMAS2D(V)
5
% Uses a linear system S, and computes S*V to get d/dy,
6
% then V*S' to get d/dx, and finally S*V*S' to get d^2/dxdy.
7
%
8
% [derivatives] = GPUTHOMAS2D(V, approx_dim1)
9
% If approx_dim1 == true, the values d/dy are approximated by a
10
% convolution: conv2(V, s, 'same'), which can give a performance boost.
11
%
12
% Note: The system S is memoized to increase performance. This means
13
% that GPU memory can leak if variablying sized inputs are used.
14
15
if nargin<=1
16
approx_dim1 = false;
17
end
18
19
% Set up cached objects. Instead of solving the matrix equations every
20
% function call, we store the inverted matrix. GEMM is fast(er) on GPU.
21
persistent getpreandc_memoized
22
persistent approx_filter
23
if isempty(getpreandc_memoized)
24
getpreandc_memoized = memoize(@getpreandc);
25
end
26
if isempty(approx_filter)
27
% An approximate filter, which computes the matrix product for
28
% derivatives in the axial direction as a simple convolution.
29
kk = 16+1; % 16 (single precision) or 24 (double precision)
30
se = @(kk) (sqrt(3)/6)*(-2+sqrt(3)).^(abs((1:kk*2-1)-kk));
31
te = conv(se(kk), [3, 0, -3],'valid');
32
approx_filter = gpuArray(single(te));
33
end
34
35
% Retrieve memoized objects.
36
if ~approx_dim1
37
% No need to retrieve matrix if we are not going to use it.
38
matr1 = getpreandc_memoized(size(V, 1));
39
end

matr2 = getpreandc_memoized(size(V, 2));

% So many ways to right-multiply matrices in MATLAB:
43
% 1) x * matr2'; (R2006a)
44
% And when we want multiple outputs:
45
% 2) pagefun(@mtimes, x, matr2'); (R2013b)
46
% 3) pagemtimes(x, 'none', matr2, 'transpose'); (R2020b)
47
% 4) gpucoder.batchedMatrixMultiply(R, matr2, dR1, matr2, 'transpose', 'NT');
48
% 5) gpucoder.stridedMatrixMultiply(cat(4, R, dR1), matr2, 'transpose', 'NT')
; (R2020a)
49
% 6) tensorprod (R2022a)
50
% (1) Is the fastest, but due to various overhead it may be slower when
51
% executed in a loop.
52
53
% For left-multiply we have an additional way of doing it by
54
% matricizing the tensor, and just carrying out the product:

rmult = @(x) x * matr2';
% This is faster than pre-transposing. I guess
MATLAB does the matrix-transposematrix-product in one BLAS call.
78
if ~ismatrix(V)
79
rmult = @(x) pagefun(@mtimes, x, matr2');
80
end
81
82
% Left-multiplication can always be done in a batched manner.
83
lmult = @(x) reshape(matr1 * reshape(x, size(x, 1), []), size(x));
84
% ^ equal to @(x) matr1 * x when not batched.
85
if approx_dim1
86
% Approximation by convolution.
87
lmult = @(x) convn(x, approx_filter', 'same');
88
end
89
90
if isreal(V)
91
[dVdx, dVdy, dVdxdy] = deriv(V, lmult, rmult);
92
derivatives = {dVdx, dVdy, dVdxdy};
93
else
94
% It turns out to be fastest to split into real and complex parts,
95
% and then just multiply each part separately by the real matrices
96
% matr1 and matr2. The split is just 3.5% of time spent.
97
% Probably because BLAS does not support real-times-complex
98
% matrix products. (We could look into CUTLASS and custom impl. it)
99
if approx_dim1
100
dV1 = lmult(V);
% <- Complex.
101
[R, I] = arrayfun(@split_complex, V);
102
dR2 = rmult(R);
103
dI2 = rmult(I);
104
[dR1, dI1] = arrayfun(@split_complex, dV1);
105
dR3 = rmult(dR1);
106
dI3 = rmult(dI1);
107
else
108
% Split to real and imaginary parts.
109
[R, I] = arrayfun(@split_complex, V);
110
[dR1, dR2, dR3] = deriv(R, lmult, rmult);
111
% clear R
% Save memory
112
[dI1, dI2, dI3] = deriv(I, lmult, rmult);
113
end
114
% Now we have derivatives in FAR SPLIT format:
115
derivatives = {dR1, dI1, dR2, dI2, dR3, dI3};
116
% FAR because we store d/dx, d/dy, d^2/dxdy in separate tensors.
==PAGE:109==
B.3. Implementation of Spline Interpolation
91
117
% SPLIT because we separate the real and imaginary parts as well.
118
% A terrible format in terms of cache locality. Results in abysmal
119
% spline evaluation performance. But if it is only evaluated once,
120
% it is not worth it to spend any time re-ordering the derivatives.
121
end
122 end
123
124 function [dfdx, dfdy, dfdxdy] = deriv(f, lmult, rmult)
125
dfdx = lmult(f);
126
dfdy = rmult(f);
127
dfdxdy = rmult(dfdx);
128 end
129
130 function [r, i] = split_complex(C)
131
r = real(C);
132
i = imag(C);
133 end
134
135 function [prod, pre, C] = getpreandc(n)
136
% GETPREANDC computes the system S used to get derivatives.
137
% [prod, pre, C] = GETPREANDC(n) computes inv(C)*P for a system of size
138
% n, and also returns P and C.
139
% and C as additional output arguments.
140
% Inspired by built-ins spline and parallel.internal.flowthrough.spline
141
% (MATLAB 2021b). These find derivatives y' of y by solving: C*y'=pre*y
142
if n < 4
143
% Spline interpolation with not-a-knot end-conditions require four
144
% points. Other cubic splines usually require at least three.
145
error('Need at least four points in each dimension for spline
interpolation.');
146
end
147
pre = eye(n, 'double');
148
prediff = diff(pre, [], 1);
149
pre(1, :)=((1+4)*prediff(1, :)+prediff(2, :))/2;
150
pre(2:n-1, :) = convn(prediff(1:n-1, :), [3; 3], 'valid');
151
pre(n, :) = (prediff(n-2, :)+(1+4)*prediff(n-1, :))/2;
152
153
C = spdiags([[ones(n-2, 1);2;0]
...
154
[1;4*ones(n-2, 1);1] ...
155
[0;2;ones(n-2, 1)] ], [-1 0 1], n, n);
156
prod = cast(full(double(C))\pre, 'like', zeros(1, 1, 'single', 'gpuArray'));
157 end

B.6
Interfacing with and compiling kernels
MATLAB re-compiles the kernel every time a CUDAKernel is initialized. Therefore a function
was made to memoize these.
1 function kern = get_kernel(kernel_name, reload)
2 %GET_KERNEL is a cache of CUDA kernels. Memoizes kernels used in various
==PAGE:114==
96
Appendix B.
MATLAB Code
3 % modules, since initializing a parallel.gpu.CUDAKernel is expensive.
4 %
5 % GET_KERNEL(kernel_name) returns the kernel with the given name.
6 %
7 % GET_KERNEL(kernel_name, reload)
8 % If a change is made to the .ptx code, kernels must be reloaded to reflect this.
9 % If reload=true, all kernels are reloaded. Short form is: GET_KERNEL('', 1).
10
11 % Version 1.0, May 1 2022, Sebastian Kazmarek Præsius
12
13 % The reason to not use the built-in "memoize" function was that memoize
14 % returns a local map object, but we want the map to be globally available,
15 % as well as handle the setup of the map.
16
17 if nargin == 1
18
reload = false;
19 end
20
21 % To prevent loading kernels every time they are used, we cache them.
22 % When they are loaded the first time they are compiled for the GPU, much like a
shader, and this is expensive.
23 persistent kernel_cache
24
25 if ~isempty(kernel_cache) && ~reload
26
% ^ Put && false to force re-load of all kernels if a change is made.
27
28
% Load kernel from cache.
29
kern = kernel_cache(kernel_name);
30
return;
31 end

A script was made to compile the kernels using the MATLAB-shipped nvcc.
Listing B.4: The script compile_kernels.m
1 % Script to compile CUDA kernels to ptx and mex files.
2 % It attempts to use the NVCC compiler shipped with MATLAB to minimize the
3 % maintenance needed (especially for mex code), making it self-contained.
4 %
5 % Mex can contain C/C++, PTX, and possibly lower level GPU assembly. It is
6 % platform specific, and possibly also MATLAB-version specific.
7 %
8 % PTX is an intermediate assembly for a virtual GPU architecture, which is
9 % then compiled to the specific GPU by its driver the first time it is ran.
10 % Therefore it is way more portable.
11 %
12 % Version 1.0, May 1 2022, Sebastian Kazmarek Præsius
13
14 % We have tried to generally use only files shipped with MATLAB, such that
15 % this compilation will work even if the user has not installed NVCC, but
16 % has only installed the MATLAB Parallel Toolbox.
17
18 nvcc_flags = '-lineinfo';
19
20 % Put in the correct cuda path.
21 % cuda_path = '/usr/local/cuda-11.5';
22 cuda_path = fullfile(matlabroot, 'sys', 'cuda', computer('arch'), 'cuda');
23
24 % Some fixes for MATLAB-shipped nvcc ptx compilation.
25 includes = {fullfile(matlabroot, 'extern', 'include'), fullfile(matlabroot, '
toolbox', 'parallel', 'gpu', 'extern', 'include', 'gpu'), fullfile(cuda_path,
'include')};
26 libdevice_dir = fullfile(matlabroot, 'bin', computer('arch'));
27 cicc_dir = fullfile(cuda_path, 'nvvm', 'bin');
28 nvcc_path = fullfile(cuda_path, 'bin', 'nvcc');
29
30 strfun = @(x, pre) cell2mat(join(cellfun(@(y) [pre '"' y '"'], x, 'UniformOutput'
, false), ' '));
31 includes_str = strfun(includes, '-I');
==PAGE:116==
98
Appendix B. MATLAB Code
32 libs_str = strfun({cicc_dir}, '-L');
33 nvcc_flags_ptx = [nvcc_flags ' --dont-use-profile -ldir "' libdevice_dir '" '
includes_str ' ' libs_str ' '];
34
35 % Add cicc to PATH environment variable.
36 env_path = getenv('PATH');
37 if ~contains(env_path, cicc_dir)
38
% Add cicc to the path of child processes (PTX compilation).
39
setenv('PATH', [env_path pathsep cicc_dir]);
40 end
41
42 % Mexcuda probably also apply these "fixes", which it loads from XML files in:
43 % fullfile(matlabroot,'toolbox','parallel','gpu','extern','src','mex',computer('
arch'))
44 % unfortunately mexcuda does not seem to support .ptx compilation, and the
45 % XML parser used is not open source, so we had to roll out our own code.
46
47 compile_ptx = @(name) system(cell2mat(join({['"', nvcc_path, '"'], nvcc_flags_ptx
, '-ptx', name})));
48 compile_mex = @(name, varargin) mexcuda('-R2018a', ['NVCC_FLAGS="' nvcc_flags '"'
], varargin{:}, name);
49 % Mex is needed in case we want to use libraries such as cuBLAS, or API.
50

36 auto calc_distance_2d(const Ty2& x0, const Ty2& x1) -> decltype(x0.x) {
37

38 }

Ty - calculation type (single/double)
47
*
48
* Returns the interpolated value
49
*
50
* Takes arguments in order
51
*
y0 - Td, y-value of first data point
52
*
y1 - Td, y-value of second data point
53
*
y2 - Td, y-value of third data point
54
*
y3 - Td, y-value of fourth data point
55
*
x0 - Ty, x-value of first data point
56
*
x - Ty, point at which interpolated value is desired
57
*/
58 template <typename Td, typename Ty>
59 __host__ __device__ inline
60 Td local_cubic_interp_fixed(const Td y0, const Td y1, const Td y2,
61
const Td y3, const Ty x0, const Ty x) {
62
const Ty dx0 = x - x0, dx1 = dx0 - 1, dx2 = dx0 - 2, dx3 = dx0 - 3;
63
return (dx1*dx2)*(dx0*y3 - dx3*y0)/((Ty) 3) + (dx0*dx3)*(dx2*y1 - dx1*y2);
64 }

INTERPOLATION END */

69 /* Calculate apodization values given the relative x and z coordinates
70
* of the element to the image point, an f-number, and a sampled
71
* apodization profile. Linear interpolation is performed in the
72
* apodization profile.
73
*
74
* Templated on
75
*
Ty - base calculation type
76
*
77
* Returns the apodization value
78
*
79
* Takes input in order
80
*
dx - Ty, relative x coordinate
81
*
dz - Ty, relative z coordinate
82
*
f - Ty, F-number
83
*
apo - Ty*, sampled apodization profile
84
*
n_samples_apo - unsigned int, number of samples in apo
85
*/
86 template <typename Ty>
87 __host__ __device__ inline
88 Ty base_calc_apodization(const Ty dx, const Ty dz, const Ty f,
89
const Ty *apo, const unsigned int n_samples_apo) {
90
// Calculate apodization index (-0.5 to 0.5 is within window)
91
Ty apo_index = dx*f/dz;
92
93
// If dz==0 and dx!=0 apo_index is inf, change here to 1
94
apo_index = dz == 0 ? 1 : apo_index;
95
96
// If dx==dz==0 apo_index is NaN but should actually be right in the
==PAGE:119==
C.1.
The original beamforming kernel
101
97
// middle of the window
98
apo_index = dx == 0 && dz == 0 ? 0 : apo_index;
99
100
// Perform linear interpolation
101
// Calculate fractional sample idx
102
const Ty sample_index = (apo_index + (Ty) 0.5) * (n_samples_apo - 1);
103
int s0 = floor(sample_index);
104
// Limit to array bounds
105
// Change here to min/max
106
s0 = max((int) 0, s0);
107
s0 = min(s0, (int) n_samples_apo-2);
108
/*
109
s0 = s0 < 0 ? 0 : s0;
110
s0 = s0 >= n_samples_apo ? n_samples_apo-2 : s0;
111
*/
112
const unsigned int s1 = s0 + 1;
113
const Ty frac_index = sample_index - s0;
114
Ty apo_val = frac_index * apo[s1] + (1 - frac_index) * apo[s0];
115
116
// Set value to 0 for extrapolated values
117
apo_val = apo_index < -0.5 || apo_index > 0.5 ? 0 : apo_val;
118
apo_val = f==0 ? 1 : apo_val;
119
120
// Return apodization value
121
return apo_val;
122 }

* coordinate, an f-number, and a sampled apodization profile. Linear

* interpolation is performed in the apodization profile.

* Templated on
129
*
Ty2 - 2-element calculation type
130
*
Ty - base calculation type

133

143 template <typename Ty2, typename Ty>
144 __host__ __device__ inline
145 Ty calc_rx_apodization(const Ty2 pos, const Ty2 img_coord,
146
const Ty f,
147
const Ty *apo,

const unsigned int n_samples_apo,
149
const Ty manual,

// Calculate the relative position of element to image point
152

154
const Ty dx = rel_pos.x;
155

156
const Ty r2 = min(lid, r1);
157

return r2;

161 /* Calculate apodization values given an element and an image point
162
* coordinate, an f-number, a sampled apodization profile, and a steering
163
* direction. Linear interpolation is performed in the apodization profile.
164
*
165

166

Ty2 - 2-element calculation type
167
*
Ty - base calculation type
168

169
* Returns the apodization value
170
*
171

174

cos_angle - Ty, cosine of steering angle

sin_angle - Ty, sine of steering angle

n_samples_apo - unsigned int, number of samples in apo

180 template <typename Ty2, typename Ty>
181 __host__ __device__ inline
182 Ty calc_tx_apodization(const Ty2 pos, const Ty2 img_coord,
183
const Ty f,
184
const Ty cos_angle,
185
const Ty sin_angle,
186
const Ty *apo,
187
const unsigned int n_samples_apo) {
188
// Calculate the relative position of element to image point
189
const Ty2 rel_pos = { pos.x - img_coord.x, pos.y - img_coord.y };
190
const Ty dx = rel_pos.x * cos_angle - rel_pos.y * sin_angle;
191
const Ty dz = rel_pos.x * sin_angle + rel_pos.y * cos_angle;
192
return base_calc_apodization<Ty>(dx, dz, f, apo, n_samples_apo);
193 }

197 /* Main beamforming kernel. All coordinates/times are prescaled such that
198
* calculations are made in "index space", i.e., spatial coordinates are
199
* scaled by fs/c while times are scaled by fs. All distance calculations
200
* are done in 2D requiring line geometries to be passed twice (once for

* TX and once for RX). Since 2-element types are used, this means .x may

326

327

In MATLAB, the float2 kernel inputs are complex numbers. It does not really matter in
terms of use; one can pass a float2 (complex single) array of size [S1,S2,...], or simply pass
a float (single) array of [2,S1,S2,...]. Either type of pointer can be cast to the the other.
We suggest using the C++ construct static_cast for this casting (not done in the code
shown), as it may give a compile-time error if something is wrong. This is because there are
certain alignment requirements for float2, which provide some beneﬁts, e.g. float2 can be
loaded by a single 64-bit load instruction. If these are not satisﬁed, the program may crash.
It likely does not make a diﬀerence for performance which format the arguments are given
in. These inputs are eventually cast to float2, and from that, the compiler just assumes/infers
alignment requirements are satisﬁed. (the converse would give undeﬁned behaviour).

14 template <typename Ty2, typename Ty>
15 __host__ __device__ inline
16 Ty check_rx_apodization(const Ty2 pos, const Ty2 img_coord, const Ty f) {
17
// Fast check, to see if we are outside the acceptance angle.
18
const Ty2 rel_pos = { pos.x - img_coord.x, pos.y - img_coord.y };
19
const Ty dz = rel_pos.y;
20
const Ty dx = rel_pos.x;
21
22
// Calculate apodization index (-0.5 to 0.5 is within window)
23

24
25
// If dz==0 and dx!=0 apo_index is inf, change here to 1
26

27

// If dx==dz==0 apo_index is NaN but should actually be right in the
middle of

29

32
// apo_val = f==0 ? 1 : apo_val;
33
34

C.4.
Software Pipelining
109
C.4
Software Pipelining
We simply implemented with manual prologue and epilogue for proﬁling purposes. Note how
large the epilogue ends up being, since it essentially requires we copy the whole loop body.
Later the epilogue is made redundant, since if shared memory is used for loading rx_pos,
we can simply ask for two more entries worth of space, and then safely load this unitialized
memory. Similarly, in the prologue there are no bound checks, uninitalized variables can be
used without consequence.
This code was not tested with Nr ≤2, so access violation may happen on line 55. This
is not an issue in the later versions, that use shared memory.
1 __global__
2 void beamform_pipelined(...) {
3
4
// Figure out which sample to calculate
5
const auto
sample_idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;

28 __device__ inline
29 float2 atomicAdd(float2 * address, const float2 val) {
30
// 2D atomicAdd for complex numbers.
31
// The two returned values are not used, but note that they may be out of
sync with one another, since only components are atomically accumulated.
32
return {atomicAdd(&address[0].x, val.x), atomicAdd(&address[0].y, val.y)};
33 }
34
35 template <typename Td, typename Ty2, typename Ty, int batch_size>
36 __host__ __device__ inline
37 void beamform_kernel_batched(...) {
38
// Make sure we're within bounds
39
if (!((line_idx < n_lines) & (sample_idx < n_samples_per_line)))

// Calculate transmit path

48
const Ty tx_pt_sign = tx_img_coord.y > tx_pos.y ? 1 : -1;
49
const Ty tx_pt_dist = tx_pt_sign*calc_distance_2d<Ty2>(tx_pos, tx_img_coord);

The code was developed earlier than some simpliﬁcations to the beamformer. The handling
of interleaved sequences could be handled by the code in listing C.2 by feeding beamforming
the individual LRIs, and simply changing the output argument depending on which HRI
they belong to.
It was time-consuming to write the mex impementation (listing C.6) re-
quired for using the CUDA Streams API (not supported in MATLAB), which is why this
implementation was not re-written or simpliﬁed. It is just for demonstration purposes.

float value = READ2D(0, 0);
49
bool isPeak = value > threshold &&
50
// Last check is if we are local maximum.
51
CRIT2D(-1, -1) && CRIT2D(0, -1) && CRIT2D(1, -1) &&
52
CRIT2D(-1,
0) && true
&& CRIT2D(1,
0) &&
53
CRIT2D(-1,
1) && CRIT2D(0,
1) && CRIT2D(1,
1);
54
55
if(!isPeak)
56
continue;
57
58
float2 dtop = make_float2(READ2D(-1, 0), READ2D(0, -1)) - make_float2
(READ2D(1, 0), READ2D(0, 1));
59
float2 dbot = make_float2(READ2D(-1, 0), READ2D(0, -1)) + make_float2
(READ2D(1, 0), READ2D(0, 1)) - 2.0f*value;
60
61
//Ensure that the interpolation is less then 0.5 as it else
62
// is a poor location, probably with multiple scatteres within
63
// the resolution cell
64
float2 dpos = dtop / (2.0f*dbot);
// TODO: Protect for NaN?
65
if(dpos.x*dpos.x + dpos.y*dpos.y <= 0.5f) {
66
int out_atomic = atomicAdd(n_peaks, 1);
67
reinterpret_cast<float2*>(out)[out_atomic] = make_float2(id.x, id
.y) + 1.0f + dpos;
68
}
69
}
70 }
71
72 __global__ void setSRI(float * __restrict__ sri, const float * __restrict__
_peakpos, const int xs, const int ys, const int npeaks) {
73
const float2 *peakpos = reinterpret_cast<const float2 *>(_peakpos);
// Is
the restrict placement really correct?
74
// TODO: Make a version that adds a gaussian shape to SRI image?
75
76
for (int i = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; i < npeaks; i += blockDim
.x * gridDim.x) {
77
float2 pp = peakpos[i] - 1.0f;
// Undo MATLAB one-based index.
78
int xc = lroundf(pp.x);
79
int yc = lroundf(pp.y);
80
if(!(0 <= xc && 0 <= yc && xc < xs && yc < ys))
81
continue;
// Peak outside of image... just ignore.
82
83
// Add the peak to the SRI.
84
// Make sure to do so atomically. (This was not handled in the original
make_peak_detections.m, which may have issues with two peaks that
coincide in position)
85
atomicAdd(sri + xc + xs * yc, 1.0f);
86
}
87 }
